The Inseparability of Power and Will and the Impossibility of Two Gods
The author proves the inseparability of divine power and will to refute the possibility of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power (qudra) and will (irāda) are inseparable and how this proves that two gods cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Power and will are inseparable; conflict in one means conflict in both, so two gods are impossible.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi builds a key proof for tawḥīd by showing that divine power and will are necessarily linked. The author argues that if two gods existed, their powers would conflict, leading to inability — and since will is not distinct from power, conflict in will follows necessarily. This lesson unpacks the argument, clarifying how Quranic verses are interpreted and why mere wish (tamannī) cannot be attributed to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Note the Quranic verses cited and the author's reasoning flow.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the argument.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where the author states that power and will are inseparable (mutalāzimān). Understand that if power is established, will is necessarily established too.
Look for the phrase 'as is the rule for inseparable correlates.'
3
Follow the proof for two gods
The author argues that if two gods existed, their powers would conflict, leading to inability (ʿajz). Since will is not distinct from power, conflict in will also occurs, making both gods incapable — impossible for lordship.
4
Understand the distinction from wish
The author distinguishes true will from mere wish (tamannī), which is a sign of inability and not attributable to Allah. This reinforces that divine will is perfect and inseparable from power.
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini is cited to support this argument.
5
Reflect on the theological conclusion
The impossibility of two gods is proven because their powers and wills would conflict, leading to deficiency. This argument strengthens the Islamic belief in absolute divine unity (tawḥīd).
The passage ends with 'And Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآية، وكذا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن يَمْسَسْكَ اللهُ بِضُرٍ فَلَا كَاشِفَ لَهُ إِلَّا هُوَ ()، فأما الاستدلال بقوله: ﴿وَإِن يُردّكَ يغير ( (٣) وكذا بقوله تعالى إِنْ أَرَادَنِيَ اللَّهُ بِضُي ) (۳) وقوله أَوْ أَرَادَنِي بِرَحْمَةٍ (٤) فالمراد من ذلك هو الفعل لا الإرادة ألا ترى أنه قال: . ،(٥) هَلْ هُنَّ كَشِفَتُ ضُرِّهِ } () ، هَلْ هُنَ مُمْسِكَتُ رَحْمَتِهِ () وإنما يحتاج إلى الكشف والإمساك بعد الفعل لا بعد الإرادة فإذا كان التعليق بالقدرة وإثبات العجز غيْر، إذ تنفيذ القدرة لن يكون بدون الإرادة، فكانت هي ثابتة ضرورة بثبوت القدرة على ما هو الحكم في المتلازمين، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أن خروج المقدور عن القدرة يكون عن عجز، وذلك منافي للربوبية، فكانت (۷) من ضرورة وقوع التعارض في القدرة (لو) كان اثنين وقوع التعارض في الإرادة إذ هي لا تمتاز عن القدرة ) (۸) وكل ما يمتاز عن القدرة فليس (۹) بإرادة بل هو تَمَنِّ والقديم غير موصوف به لأنه من أمارات العجز ، ولهذا قال أبو إسحاق (۱۰) الأسفراييني بعدما حكي اعتراض أبي هاشم على الإرادة: وليس هذا وجه الاستدلال به لكنه ما قدمنا من تحقيق القدرة (۱۱) ونفي النقيصة ثم يرتقي (۱۲) منه إلى الإرادة أي لما ثبت التعارض في القدرة وتعطلت قدرتهما أو قدرة أحدهما ، ثبت ذلك في الإرادة ضرورة استحالة امتيازهما إذ ما امتازت عنه الإرادة اضطرارا وليس بقدرة (١) ، والله الموفق.
The verse, and likewise by His statement: {And if Allah touches you with harm, there is no remover of it except Him} (Qur'an 10:107). As for the argument by His statement: {And if He intends you some harm} (Qur'an 39:38) and likewise by His statement: {If Allah intends me with harm} (Qur'an 39:38) and His statement: {or intends me with mercy} (Qur'an 39:38), the intended meaning of that is the action, not the will. Do you not see that He said: {Are they the removers of His harm?} (Qur'an 39:38), {Are they the withholders of His mercy?} (Qur'an 39:38). And need for removal and withholding is only after the action, not after the will. So when the conditional clause is linked to power and the affirmation of inability is not possible, because the execution of power cannot be without will, so it (will) is necessarily established by the establishment of power, as is the rule for inseparable correlates. And we have adduced the proof that the exiting of the object of power from power occurs due to inability, and that is incompatible with lordship. So it (i.e., will) is necessarily from the necessity of the occurrence of conflict in power were there two (gods). The occurrence of conflict in will (is also necessary) because it (will) is not distinct from power. And everything that is distinct from power is not will but rather wish, and the Eternal is not described by it because it is one of the signs of inability. For this reason Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said, after narrating the objection of Abu Hashim regarding will: 'This is not the way of argument by it, but rather what we have presented of the verification of power and the denial of deficiency, then one ascends from it to will.' That is, when conflict in power is established and their power becomes incapacitated, or the power of one of them, that is established in will necessarily, because it is impossible for them to be distinct, since whatever the will is distinct from is necessarily not power. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a proof of tawhid (divine unity) by demonstrating the necessary connection between divine power (qudra) and will (irāda). It begins by citing Quranic verses that speak of Allah's power to remove harm or grant mercy. The author argues that these verses refer to actions, not will, because the ability to remove or withhold only makes sense after an action exists. However, the conditional link (taʿlīq) in these verses is actually tied to power (qudra), and since the execution of power requires will, the existence of will is necessarily implied by the existence of power—they are inseparable correlates (mutalāzimān). If there were two gods, their powers would conflict, leading to the inability of one or both to act, which is impossibility (ʿajz) and incompatible with divinity (rubūbiyya). Because will is not distinct from power (lā tamtāzu ʿan al-qudra), conflict in power necessitates conflict in will. Any supposed will that is distinct from power is actually mere wish (tamannī), which is a sign of inability and not attributable to the Eternal. The author then cites Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who states that the correct argument begins by establishing the reality of power and denying any deficiency, then ascends to will. The conclusion: if conflict in power is proven, conflict in will follows necessarily, because the two attributes cannot be separated. The passage ends with 'And Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
قدرة (qudra)
تعارض (taʿāruḍ)
عجز (ʿajz)
ربوبية (rubūbiyya)
متلازمين (mutalāzimayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will can be considered independently of divine power.
Correction: Will and power are inseparable; conflict in power necessarily entails conflict in will, so if two gods had conflicting powers, their wills would also conflict, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why must conflict in will necessarily follow from conflict in power?
A) Because will is independent of power.
B) Because will is not distinct from power.
C) Because will is a sign of ability.
D) Because power is derived from will.

Key Takeaway
The inseparability of divine power and will shows that two gods cannot exist, as their powers would conflict and lead to inability, which contradicts divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation beyond your control, do you truly believe that no one but Allah can remove harm or grant mercy? Or do you secretly rely on other means as if they had independent power? Reflect on a recent difficulty—did your heart turn to Allah alone, or did you look to others as if they could change your fate? Commit to making your du'a exclusive to Allah today.
?
The lesson shows that any separation between will and power reduces will to mere wish. Examine your own intentions: are your desires backed by the conviction that Allah alone has power to fulfill them, or are they empty wishes unaccompanied by trust in His qudra? What wish can you transform into a sincere supplication today?
?
Two gods are impossible because conflict leads to inability. In your own life, when you try to serve multiple masters—pleasing people, seeking status, chasing desires—does that inner conflict render you spiritually powerless? How can you realign your will to single-hearted devotion to Allah, the only One whose power is without deficiency?

The Magian Shubha and Its Refutation
Nasafi presents the Magian argument for dualism and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Magian argument for dualism based on the existence of evil, and how Nasafi refutes it using the conditions of eternity and the concept of mutual prevention.

Core Idea
Evil does not prove a second eternal creator; deficiency cannot be eternal.

The Magians, an ancient dualist sect, argued that the presence of evil and imperfection in the world proves the existence of a second, evil creator alongside Allah. Nasafi presents their reasoning and then systematically dismantles it, showing that eternity requires perfection and that deficiency indicates dependence on another being. This lesson examines both the shubha and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Magian shubha
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how they argue from the existence of harmful, ugly things to a foolish originator (Ahriman).
They assume that a wise creator cannot create evil.
2
Examine the refutation's first point
Nasafi begins by reaffirming that there is no other eternal being with Allah. This is based on prior proofs. Understand that this eliminates the possibility of an eternal Ahriman.
3
Understand the condition of eternity
The text states that one condition of eternity is perfection. A deficient being cannot be eternal. Ask yourself: why can't an eternal being be foolish or imperfect?
Deficiency would imply the possibility of non-existence.
4
Follow the argument about deficiency and another
If deficiency occurs in an essence, it must be due to another being, so that the other's perfection is known through contrast. This refutes the Magian claim.
5
Consider the additional argument on originated Ahriman
If Ahriman were originated (as some Magians held), he could not have power to create, because two creators would lead to mutual prevention (tamānu'), which is impossible.
This argument is referenced from earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة المجوس في إثنينيية الخالق: وشبهتهم أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو ضار قبيح مستقذر، وإيجاد هذه الأشياء ليس بحكمه، والباري حكيم فلن يجوز منه إيجاد هذه الأشياء، لما أنه يستحيل عليه السفه ولا شك أنها محدثة، فلابد لها من محدث، فعلمنا أن لها محدثا سفيها وهو أهرمن، وكذا موجد الشر شرير والباري جل وعلا منزه عن هذا الوصف، فدل أن وجود الشر كان بإيجاد غيره لا بإيجاده. مناقشة كلامهم: إلا أنا نقول: إن ما أقمنا من الدلالة موجب بطلان أن يكون مع الله تعالى قديم آخر، فبطل القول بقدم اثنين ولأن القول بقدم السفيه ناقص محال، إذ من شرط القدم الكمال لأن ذاتا ما لا يوجب نقصه لا في ذاته ولا في صفاته، إذ ذلك اقتضاء عدمه، وذات ما لا يقتضي عدمه، إذ لو اقتضاه لما تصور وجوده فإذا كان نقص تمكن في ذات ما فذلك بإثبات غيره، ليعرف بنقصان هذا كمال مثبته، ولو كان أهرمن محدثا على قول القائلين: إنه محدث، فلا ينبغي أن يكون له قدرة الإيجاد (لما فيه) من إثبات التمانع، وقد بينا فساد ذلك.
The doubt of the Magians concerning the duality of the Creator: Their doubt is that among the parts of the world there is what is harmful, ugly, and filthy, and bringing these things into existence is not according to wisdom, and the Creator is Wise, so it is not permissible for Him to bring these things into existence, because foolishness is impossible for Him; and there is no doubt that they are originated, so they must have an originator, so we know that they have a foolish originator, and he is Ahriman. Likewise, the originator of evil is evil, and the Creator, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above this description; so it indicates that the existence of evil was by the bringing into existence of another, not by His bringing into existence. Discussion of their statement: Except we say: Indeed, what we have established of evidence necessitates the invalidity of there being another eternal with Allah, so the statement of the eternity of two is invalidated. And because the statement of the eternity of the foolish one is deficient, impossible, for one of the conditions of eternity is perfection; because an essence that does not necessitate its deficiency neither in its essence nor in its attributes—since that would imply its non-existence—and an essence that does not imply its non-existence, because if it implied it, its existence would be inconceivable. So when deficiency attaches to an essence, that is by the affirmation of another, so that by the deficiency of this one the perfection of its establisher may be known. And if Ahriman were originated, according to the saying of those who say he is originated, then he should not have the power of bringing into existence, because of what is in it of affirming mutual prevention (al-tamānuʿ), and we have already shown the corruption of that.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the Magian argument (shubha) for dualism and its refutation. The Magians argue that since the world contains harmful, ugly, and filthy things, a wise Creator cannot be their originator, because it would entail foolishness (safah) which is impossible for Him. These things are originated, so they must have an originator, and that originator must be foolish, i.e., Ahriman. Similarly, the originator of evil must be evil, and God is transcendent above that. Thus, evil originates from another being. The refutation begins by restating the previously established proof that there cannot be another eternal being alongside Allah. Therefore, the claim of two eternals is invalid. Furthermore, the notion of an eternal being that is foolish (nāqiṣ) is impossible, because one of the conditions of eternity is perfection (kamāl). An essence that does not necessitate deficiency in itself or its attributes—because that would imply its non-existence—and an essence that does not imply its non-existence, because if it did, its existence would be inconceivable. So when deficiency occurs in an essence, it is due to the affirmation of another being, so that through the deficiency of this one the perfection of its establisher may be known. Additionally, if Ahriman were originated (as some Magians claim), then he should not have the power to create, because that would involve mutual prevention (al-tamānuʿ), which has already been shown to be corrupt.

Key Terms
شبهة
إثنينية
سفيه
ناقص
كمال
قدم
التمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil proves there is a second, evil creator alongside Allah.
Correction: Evil does not require a second creator because: (1) prior proofs show no other eternal being with Allah; (2) eternity requires perfection, so a deficient eternal being is impossible; (3) deficiency in a created being points to its dependence on another, not to two creators.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is one of the conditions of eternity?
A) Foolishness
B) Imperfection
C) Perfection
D) Origination

Key Takeaway
The Magian argument for dualism fails because eternity requires perfection and deficiency points to a single Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter evil or hardship in your life, do you ever find yourself unconsciously attributing it to a power other than Allah, as if evil has its own independent reality? How does this lesson's refutation of dualism challenge that tendency? What can you do this week to remind yourself that all events come from the One Perfect Creator?
?
Think of a recent trial you faced. Did you question God's wisdom or feel that He was being unfair? How does the truth that deficiency points to perfection—meaning your shortcomings reveal His completeness—change the way you view your own failures? Will you commit to seeing every difficulty as a sign of His perfection rather than a flaw in His plan?
?
The Magians assumed that a wise Creator cannot create evil. Have you ever secretly held a similar assumption, believing that good things come from Allah and evil from somewhere else? How can you actively train your heart to see that everything—including what you dislike—is from the All-Wise, and that nothing occurs outside His perfect will? Name one specific action you will take to strengthen this conviction.

Refuting the Dualist Objection about Evil
Imam Nasafi refutes the dualist claim that evil cannot come from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the dualist objection by demonstrating that their own doctrine of an eternal light leads to logical absurdities, and that evil can be attributed to God's will without compromising His wisdom.

Core Idea
Evil is not inconsistent with divine wisdom; dualists' own beliefs entail greater evils.

The problem of evil is a classic objection against divine wisdom. The dualists (Majūs) argued that a wise God cannot be the source of evil. Imam Nasafi responds by turning their own cosmology against them. He demonstrates that their concept of an eternal 'light' suffers from far greater imperfections, and that any attempt to avoid attributing evil to God leads to logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical style of the argument.
2
Identify the dualist objection and the first counter
The passage begins by stating the dualist claim that evil cannot come from a wise God. Imam Nasafi's first response is to point out the defects in their own concept of the ancient light.
3
Trace the three possibilities for the origin of doubt
The argument then examines three scenarios: by choice, by compulsion, or spontaneously. Each leads to a contradiction with the dualist's own assumptions.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Consider the final dilemmas about knowledge and power
Even if spontaneous occurrence were allowed, further questions show that the ancient light would be either ignorant, incapable, or complicit in evil.
The conclusion is that evil is ultimately traced to its choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجود الشر بفعل الحكيم، وخروج هذه الأجسام المستقبحة المستقذرة عن الحكمة ويستحيل على الله تعالى السفه. قلنا لهم لما جاز الجهل على النور القديم عندكم وجاز عليه الشك والهم والحاجة إلى طلب الولد، وجاز عليه العجز عن قهر عدوه حتى صالحه وهادئه جاز عليه الشر، وأي شر فوق هذه الشرور. ثم يقال لهم: تلك الشكة والفكرة التي حدث منها أهر من حدثت باختياره أم لا باختياره؟ فإن حدثت منه باختياره فمن أصل الشر، فإذا جاز منه إيجاده الشرور باختياره فلماذا لا يجوز إيجاد ما هو دونه من الشرور؟ وإن حدثت لا باختياره قيل: حدثت بفعله الاضطراري أم بنفسها لا بفعل أحد؟ فإن حدثت بفعله الاضطراري فهو إذا مضطر عاجز، إذ المضطر من يوجد غيره فيه فعلا، وكل من هو عاجز ومحل لمقدور غيره فهو محدث، فالقول إذا بقدم يزدان محال. وإن حدثت الفكرة بنفسها لا بفعل النور باختياره أو اضطراره فهو محال لما مر من استحالة حدوث حادث لا بإحداث أحد، ولو جاز ذا لجاز حدوث جميع العالم بنفسه، وبطلت الدلالة على الصانع، ثم لو وجدت الفكرة أو الشكة بنفسها هل كان القديم عالما بحدوثها أم لا؟ فإن قالوا لا فقد جهلوه، والجهل لا يليق بالصانع القديم، وإن قالوا نعم قيل لهم هل كان قادرا على منعها عن الحدوث؟ فإن قالوا لا فقد عجزوه وهو أيضا مناف للقدم، وإن قالوا نعم قيل لهم فلم لم يمنعها عن الحدوث لئلا يتولد منها ما هو أصل الشرور؟ فإذا صار الشر مضافا إلى اختياره وإرادته.
The existence of evil from the act of the Wise (al-Ḥakīm), and the coming forth of these ugly, filthy bodies [is] contrary to wisdom and foolishness (safah) is impossible for Allah. We said to them: since ignorance is permissible for the ancient light (al-nūr al-qadīm) according to you, and doubt, grief, need to seek a child, and inability to overcome his enemy until he made peace and truce with him are permissible for it, then evil is permissible for it—and what evil is greater than these evils? Then it is said to them: That doubt (shakka) and thought (fikra) which occurred from it [that cause] more [evil] than [that]—did they occur by its choice or not by its choice? If they occurred from it by its choice, then it is from the origin of evil. Since it is permissible for it to bring about evils by its choice, why is it not permissible to bring about what is less than these evils? And if they occurred not by its choice, it is said: did they occur by its compulsory act, or by themselves without anyone's act? If they occurred by its compulsory act, then it is compelled (muḍṭarr) and incapable (ʿājiz), for the compelled is one in whom another produces an act; and everyone who is incapable and a locus for another's capability is originated (muḥdath). So the statement of the eternity of Yazdan is impossible. And if the thought occurred by itself, not by the act of the light by its choice or compulsion, that is impossible due to what has passed of the impossibility of an originated event occurring without anyone's bringing it about. If this were permissible, then the origination of the entire world by itself would be permissible, and the proof of the Maker would be invalidated. Then, if the thought or doubt occurred by itself, was the ancient (al-qadīm) aware of its occurrence or not? If they say no, they have considered it ignorant, and ignorance is not befitting for the ancient Maker. If they say yes, it is said to them: was it able to prevent it from occurring? If they say no, they have considered it incapable, which is also contradictory to eternity. If they say yes, it is said to them: why did it not prevent it from occurring, lest there generate from it what is the origin of evils? Thus evil becomes attributed to its choice and will.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Majūs) objection that evil cannot come from a wise God. The argument proceeds by turning the dualists' own doctrine of an eternal 'light' (nūr qadīm) as a deity against them. First, the author states the dualists' position: they claim that the existence of evil from the act of the Wise is impossible and that ugly objects are inconsistent with wisdom. Then he responds: since according to their beliefs, their eternal light can be ignorant, doubtful, sad, in need of offspring, and unable to subdue its enemy, it is therefore capable of evil—indeed worse evils than those they object to. Next, he refines the argument by asking about the origin of the doubt and thought (shakka and fikra) in this light. Three possibilities are examined: (1) if they are voluntary, then the light directly chooses evil; (2) if they are involuntary but caused by an external compulsion, then the light is compelled and incapable, making it originated (muḥdath) not eternal; (3) if they occur spontaneously without any agent, then this leads to the impossible conclusion that events can occur without a creator, destroying the proof for the Maker. Even if spontaneous occurrence is assumed, further questions arise: was the light aware? If not, it is ignorant; if aware, could it have prevented them? If it could not, it is incapable; if it could, then why did it not prevent the root of evil? Thus the evil is ultimately traced back to its will and choice. The author concludes that the dualists cannot consistently deny that God creates evil without attributing greater evils to their own deity.

Key Terms
شر (evil)
حكيم (Wise)
نور قديم (ancient light)
شكة (doubt)
فكرة (thought)
اختيار (choice)
اضطرار (compulsion)
مضطر (compelled)
عاجز (incapable)
محدث (originated)
قدم (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refuting dualists requires proving that evil is good or that God does not create evil.
Correction: The argument shows that dualists' own doctrine leads to absurdities, so evil can be attributed to God's will without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, if the doubt in the ancient light occurred by itself without any agent, what conclusion would follow?
A) The light would be eternal.
B) It would prove that evil comes from God.
C) The entire world could originate by itself and the proof of the Maker would be invalidated.
D) The light would be incapable.

Key Takeaway
Evil can be attributed to God's will without compromising His wisdom, as shown by the logical inconsistencies in the dualist position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an evil in your own life, do you immediately question God's wisdom, or do you pause and remember that He is al-Ḥakīm? What is one concrete situation where you can train yourself to withhold judgment about the wisdom behind apparent evil?
?
The dualists attributed evils to a separate source to 'protect' God. But in doing so, they made their deity weak and ignorant. Are there areas of your faith where you try to 'protect' God by denying His complete sovereignty over all events? Are you ready to accept that everything, including suffering, is from Him and is perfectly wise?
?
The argument ends with tracing evil ultimately to the light's will. That means evil is not random but under control. How does this truth change the way you react when something painful happens? Can you commit to seeing it as part of a wise plan, even if you cannot see it yet?

The Dilemma of the Reconciler in Dualist Cosmology
Logical dilemma refuting dualist reconciler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualist claim of two eternal origins leads to absurdity through the logical dilemma of the reconciler.

Core Idea
A third reconciler between light and darkness creates an infinite regress.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes the dualist claim that good and evil arise from two eternal principles (light and darkness). He presents a logical dilemma: if there is a third reconciling principle, its nature cannot be consistently defined without contradiction or infinite regress. This argument demonstrates the rational impossibility of multiple eternal origins.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist position
Dualists claim two eternal origins (light and darkness) from which good and evil arise. The question is how they interact or reconcile.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Examine the three options for the reconciler
The author presents three possibilities: the reconciler is of both kinds, of one kind, or different from both.
Each option will be examined for logical consistency.
3
See how each option fails
If it is of both kinds, it is contradictory. If of one kind, it cannot reconcile with its opposite. If different from both, it needs its own reconciler.
The third option leads to an infinite regress.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since all options fail, the dualist position is rationally impossible. This refutes the need for a third principle.
The argument is 'clear' as the author states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفتنة من إثبات أصلين قديمين متضادين حيث جاز الخير والشر من واحد، ثم يقال لهم : الثالث المعدل أهو من جنس النور والظلمة جميعا أو هو من جنس أحدهما أو مخالف لهما جميعا؟ فإن قالوا: هو من جنسهما، فقد جعلوه نورا وظلمة معا وهو محال. وإن قالوا: هو من جنس أحدهما، قيل: فكيف صار معدلا بينهما وهو ضد لذلك الآخر؟ ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يكون المعدل من جنسه معدلا. وإن قالوا: هو مخالف لهما قيل: احتاج هو إذا إلى المعدل بينه وبينهما، لمخالفته إياها وتضاده لهما، بل إلى معدلين ليكون أحدهما بينه وبين النور والآخر بينه وبين الظلمة، ثم الكلام في المعدلين الآخرين على هذا الترتيب، وهذا واضح بحمد الله تعالى.
The trial [problem] arises from affirming two opposing eternal origins, when good and evil are possible from one [origin]. Then it is said to them: The third reconciler – is it of the same kind as light and darkness together, or of the same kind as one of them, or different from both? If they say: It is of their kind, they have made it both light and darkness together, which is impossible. If they say: It is of the same kind as one of them, it is said: How can it become a reconciler between them while it is opposite to that other? And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for a reconciler of its own kind to be a reconciler. If they say: It is different from both, it is said: Then it itself needs a reconciler between it and them, due to its difference from them and opposition to them – indeed it needs two reconcilers, so that one is between it and light, and the other between it and darkness. Then the discussion about the other reconcilers follows this order. And this is clear, praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the dualist claim that there are two eternal principles (light and darkness) from which good and evil arise. The author poses a dilemma: if there is a third principle that reconciles between them, what is its nature? If it is of both kinds, it is contradictory. If of one, it cannot be a reconciler since it is opposite to the other. If different from both, then it itself requires a reconciler, leading to an infinite regress. Thus the dualist position is absurd.

Key Terms
أصلان قديمان (two eternal origins)
نور (light)
ظلمة (darkness)
معدل (reconciler)
تضاد (opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a third reconciling principle can solve the problem of two opposing eternal origins.
Correction: The passage shows that any such reconciler leads to contradictions or an infinite regress, thus the dualist position remains irrational.

Checkpoint
What is the third option for the reconciler that leads to an infinite regress?
A) It is of the same kind as both light and darkness.
B) It is of the same kind as one of them.
C) It is different from both.
D) It does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The dualist claim of two eternal origins is logically impossible because any attempt to introduce a reconciler results in contradiction or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does your heart cling to as an 'ultimate cause' besides Allah, and how does this dilemma expose the impossibility of that belief?
?
When you find yourself trying to reconcile competing loyalties in your life, are you not falling into the same infinite regress this passage describes?
?
If Allah alone is sufficient, why do you still search for other explanations or intermediaries? What would it cost you to fully submit to that oneness?

Proof That God Is Eternal (Qadim)
Al-Nasafi proves God's eternity from the world's creation, using the impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's proof that God is eternal via the impossibility of an infinite regress of creators.

Core Idea
If God were not eternal, He would be caused, leading to an impossible infinite chain.

Al-Nasafi builds on two established truths: the world is created and there is a Creator. He then argues that the Creator must be eternal, because if He were not, He would need a creator, and so on without end—which is impossible. This passage presents a concise reductio ad absurdum in classical kalam style.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This proof assumes two premises established earlier: the world is originated (hadith) and there is a Maker (sani'). Make sure you accept these before proceeding.
The proof does not re-establish these premises; it builds on them.
2
Follow the reductio logic
The argument says: if the Maker were not eternal, He would be originated. Then He would need an originator, leading to an infinite regress of originators.
Notice the key move: 'no intermediate between eternal and originated'.
3
Grasp the impossibility
An infinite regress of actual causes is impossible because an actual infinite cannot exist. Therefore the world's existence would be impossible—but we observe it exists. Contradiction.
This is a standard argument in kalam (and later in Western philosophy).
4
Conclude the necessity
Therefore the Maker does not share the attribute of originatedness; He must be eternal by necessity (qadim bi-d-darura).
The conclusion is stated twice for emphasis.
5
Clarify terminology
Understand that 'qadim' here means 'without beginning' (eternal a parte ante), not just 'old'. Similarly, 'muhdath' means 'having a beginning'.
A common mistake is to confuse 'qadim' with 'ancient' in the ordinary sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في أن الباري عز وجل قديم: وإذا ثبت حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، علمنا أنه قديم إذ لو لم يكن قديما لكان محدثا، لما مر أن لا واسطة بينهما، ولو كان محدثا لافتقر إلى محدث، وكذا الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا يتناهى وصار حدوث العالم ووجوده متعلقا بما لا يتناهى، ومالا يتناهى لا يتصور ثبوته، فإذا لا يتصور ثبوت العالم وحدوثه، ونحن نشاهده ثابتا، ونعرف كونه حادثا بالدليل دل أن وجوده وثبوته لم يتعلق بما لا يتصور ثبوته، فدل، أن موجده لا يوافقه في صفة الحدوث، فيكون قديما ضرورة. والله الموفق.
The discourse concerning that the Creator, mighty and majestic, is eternal: And when the originatedness of the world and the existence of the Maker are established, we know that He is eternal, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated, since it has passed that there is no intermediate between them. And if He were originated, He would need an originator, and likewise the second and third ad infinitum, and the originatedness and existence of the world would become dependent on what is infinite, and what is infinite cannot be conceived to exist; then the existence and originatedness of the world cannot be conceived, while we observe it existing and we know it to be originated by proof. This indicates that its existence and subsistence are not dependent on that whose existence is inconceivable, and thus its Maker does not share with it the attribute of originatedness, so He is eternal by necessity. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God. Al-Nasafi begins with two premises that have been established: the world is originated (hadith) and there is a Maker (sani'). From these we know that the Maker is eternal (qadim). The proof proceeds as a reductio. If He were not eternal, He would be originated (muhdath), because there is no middle state between eternal and originated. If He were originated, He would need an originator (muhdith), and that originator would also need another, leading to an infinite regress. Consequently, the world's existence would depend on an infinite chain, but an infinite chain cannot actually exist. Therefore, the world's existence would be impossible, but we observe it exists. So the originator cannot be originated; He must be eternal. The argument relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. It is a standard kalam proof for the eternity of God.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal)
محدث (originated)
ما لا يتناهى (infinite)
ضرورة (by necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'qadim' simply means 'existing in the past' or 'old' rather than 'having no beginning'.
Correction: In this context, qadim means 'without beginning' (eternal a parte ante), directly opposed to muhdath (originated, having a beginning).

Checkpoint
In al-Nasafi's proof, what happens if the Creator were not eternal?
A) He would be an accident
B) He would be originated and need an originator, leading to an infinite regress
C) He would be incapable of creation
D) He would be multiple

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's proof shows that God must be eternal because an infinite regress of causes is impossible; otherwise the world's existence would be impossible, contradicting observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not eternal, your own existence would be impossible—do you live each day as if your very being depends on an uncreated, uncaused God? Where does your life betray a dependence on created things instead?
?
The proof shows that God cannot be like anything originated, yet how often do you imagine Him with human limitations? What would change if you truly believed He is utterly beyond time and causation?
?
Al-Nasafi says the world's existence depends on an infinite chain being impossible. Do you feel the weight of that? Today, when you face uncertainty, do you trust that the One who holds your existence is not subject to the chains of causality that bind you?

6 min
Proving the Creator's Eternity
Nasafi proves the Creator's eternity by reductio ad absurdum against temporal regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to prove the eternity of the Creator, based on the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes.

Core Idea
If God were temporal, the world could not exist.

This passage presents a powerful rational proof for God's eternity. Nasafi builds on the previous establishment of the world's temporality and its need for a Maker. He then shows that if the Maker were temporal like the world, we would face an impossible chain of causes stretching back infinitely. Since we observe the world exists, that chain cannot be real, so the Maker must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic source and its literal English translation. Let the argument unfold in the author's own words before diving into analysis.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Trace the conditional argument
Identify the conditional chain: if the Creator were not eternal, He would be temporal; if temporal, He would need a maker; that maker would also need a maker, leading to an infinite regress. This would make the world's existence dependent on an impossible infinite chain.
Notice the logical connector 'لو ... لـ ...' (if... then...).
3
Reflect on the reductio
Understand why the infinite regress is impossible: such a chain cannot be actualized, yet we observe the world's existence. Contradiction: the world cannot exist if its cause is dependent on an impossible regress. Therefore, the original assumption (Creator is temporal) is false, so He is eternal.
This proof relies on the established premise that an infinite regress of causes is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
۱ - الكلام في أن الباري عز وجل قديم:
وإذا ثبت حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، علمنا أنه قديم إذ لو لم يكن قديما لكان محدثا ، لما مر أن لا واسطة بينهما، ولو كان محدثا لافتقر إلى محدث، وكذا الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا يتناهى وصار حدوث العالم ووجوده متعلقا بما لا يتناهى، ومالا يتناهى لا يتصور ثبوته، فإذا لا يتصور ثبوت العالم وحدوثه، ونحن نشاهده ثابتا، ونعرف كونه حادثا بالدليل دل أن وجوده وثبوته لم يتعلق بما لا يتصور ثبوته، فدل، أن موجده لا يوافقه في صفة الحدوث، فيكون قديما ضرورة. والله الموفق.
Chapter Five. 1 - Discussion that the Creator, mighty and glorious, is eternal. And when the temporality of the world and the establishment of the Maker are proven, we know that He is eternal, because if He were not eternal, He would be temporal, since as has passed there is no intermediary between them. And if He were temporal, He would need a maker, and likewise the second and third ad infinitum, and the temporality and existence of the world would become dependent on what is infinite, and what is infinite cannot be conceived to exist. Thus, if the existence and temporality of the world cannot be conceived—yet we observe it existing and we know it to be temporal by proof—this indicates that its existence and establishment are not dependent on what cannot be conceived to exist. So it indicates that its Maker does not share with it the attribute of temporality, so He is eternal necessarily. And God grants success.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the eternity (qidam) of God. The author first states the premises: the world is temporally originated (hadith) and has a Maker (Sani'). Then he argues: if the Maker were not eternal, He would be temporal, because there is no middle state between eternal and temporal. If He were temporal, He would need another maker, and so on ad infinitum. This would make the world's existence dependent on an infinite chain, but an infinite chain cannot actually exist (since it cannot be conceived to be realized). Yet we observe the world existing and we know it is temporal through proof. Therefore, the world's existence cannot depend on an impossible infinite chain. Hence, its Maker must not be temporal like the world; rather, He must be eternal. The conclusion is that God is eternal by necessity. The proof hinges on the impossibility of an infinite regress and the observed reality of the world.

Key Terms
قديم
محدث
حدوث
ما لا يتناهى
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof merely assumes infinite regress is impossible without justification, or that it relies on a hidden premise.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that 'what is infinite cannot be conceived to exist' (ma la yatanaaha la yutasaru thubutuhu). This is a foundational principle in Kalam: an actual infinite is impossible. The proof does not assume; it uses a premise already established in the broader theological framework.

Checkpoint
What is the key intermediate step in Nasafi's proof of God's eternity?
A) The world is eternal, so its maker must be eternal.
B) If God were temporal, an infinite regress would follow, making the world's existence impossible.
C) God's essence is unknowable, so we cannot prove eternity.
D) The world was created by multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity is necessitated by the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes and the observed existence of the temporal world.

8 min
Refuting the Claim that Allah is a Substance
Refutes the claim that Allah is a substance, with a division of existents and mention of Ibn Karram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theologians reject the description of Allah as a substance (jawhar) and how the division of existents in the witnessed world supports this conclusion.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be a substance because substances are contingent and limited.

The author has already established that Allah is not an accident. He now turns to the claim that Allah is a substance. By dividing existents into accident and entity, and further into non-composite and composite, he shows that describing Allah as a substance is impossible. He also addresses the Christian argument and Ibn Karram's agreement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of existents
Notice how the author divides the existent in the witnessed world into two categories: accident (ʿaraḍ) and entity (ʿayn). Reflect on the characteristics of each.
Accidents cannot remain and cannot subsist by themselves; entities can remain and subsist by themselves.
2
Explore the further division of the entity
The entity is further divided into non-composite (jawhar) and composite (jism). The theologians call the non-composite a substance.
This division is based on composition.
3
Consider why Allah cannot be a jawhar
The author reflects and finds that describing Allah as a substance is impossible and prevented. Think about why substances in the witnessed world are contingent.
4
Learn about the Christian claim and Ibn Karram
The Christians claimed Allah is a substance, and among Muslims, only Ibn Karram agreed. Note the author's strong disapproval.
Ibn Karram is quoted as saying Allah is 'one of substance'.
5
Analyze the Christian sophistry (shubha)
The Christians argue that a substance is defined as 'subsistent by itself' (qāʾim bi-dh-dhāt), and since Allah is subsistent by Himself, He would be a substance. The passage ends here, but the refutation is implied.
This shubha is based on a flawed definition or application.

الكلام في إبطال قول من يقول : إن الباري جل وعز جوهر: وإذا ثبت استحالة كون الباري عرضا ، فبعد ذلك تأملنا فوجدنا الموجود في الشاهد، ينقسم إلى ما يستحيل بقاؤه ويمتنع قيامه بذاته وهو العرض، وإلى ما يستحيل بقاؤه، ولا يمتنع قيامه بذاته وهو العين واستحال كونه عرضا لما مر ، فعلمنا ضرورة استحالة اتصافه بكونه عرضا أنه باق، غير مفتقر إلى مكان، ثم رأينا أن ما وراء العرض في الشاهد هو العين، وهو ينقسم إلى غير متركب، وهو المسمى عند المتكلمين جوهر، وإلى متركب وهو المسمى جسما فتأملنا أنه هل يوصف بكونه جوهرا أم لا ، فوجدنا اتصافه بكونه جوهرا محالا ممتنعا. وزعمت النصارى أنه تعالى جوهر. ولم يساعدهم أحد من أهل قبلتنا إلا ابن كرام، فإن الأسفراييني ذكر في باب فضائح الكرامية من كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة، أن ابن كرام ذكر في كتابه الملقب بعذاب القبر، في وصف ذات الله تعالى، أنه أحدي الذات إحدي الجوهر، وهذا من ابن كرام خروج عن إجماع المسلمين، والتحاق بالنصارى. شبه النصارى في كونه تعالى جوهرا: قائم بالذات، وهذا حد صحيح لاطراده وانعكاسه، لأن كل جوهر في الشاهد قائم بالذات وكل قائم بالذات في الشاهد جوهر، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت تعلق كونه
The discourse on refuting the statement of one who says that the Creator, glorious and exalted, is a substance (jawhar): And when the impossibility of the Creator being an accident is established, then after that we reflected and found that the existent in the witnessed world divides into that whose remaining is impossible and whose subsistence by itself is prevented—that is the accident—and into that whose remaining is impossible but whose subsistence by itself is not prevented—that is the 'ayn (entity). And it is impossible for Him to be an accident for the reasons mentioned. So we necessarily knew from the impossibility of His being described as an accident that He is everlasting, not in need of a place. Then we saw that beyond the accident in the witnessed world is the 'ayn, and it divides into non-composite, which is called by the theologians 'substance' (jawhar), and composite, which is called 'body' (jism). So we reflected whether He can be described as a substance or not, and we found that describing Him as a substance is impossible and prevented. The Christians claimed that He, exalted, is a substance, and none of the people of our qibla supported them except Ibn Karram. For al-Isfarayini mentioned in the chapter on the scandals of the Karramiyya from his book called 'Taʿjīz al-Muʿtazila' that Ibn Karram mentioned in his book nicknamed 'ʿAdhāb al-Qabr', in describing the essence of Allah, that He is one of essence [and] one of substance. This from Ibn Karram is a departure from the consensus of the Muslims and an affiliation with the Christians. The sophistry of the Christians regarding His being a substance: [He is] subsistent by Himself, and this definition is sound in terms of its universality and converseness, because every substance in the witnessed world is subsistent by itself, and everything subsistent by itself in the witnessed world is a substance. And if that is the case, then the connection of His being ...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the claim that Allah is a substance (jawhar). The author first recalls that it is already proven that Allah is not an accident. Then he presents a division of existents in the witnessed world: (1) accident (ʿaraḍ) – that which cannot remain and cannot subsist by itself; (2) entity (ʿayn) – that which can remain but does not subsist by itself? Actually the text says 'what cannot remain but its subsistence by itself is not prevented' – this seems contradictory; likely it means the entity (ʿayn) can remain and subsists by itself. The author then states that since Allah is not an accident, He must be an entity. But then entities divide into non-composite – called jawhar (substance) – and composite – called jism (body). The author considers whether Allah can be called a jawhar and finds it impossible. He then notes that the Christians claimed Allah is a substance, and among Muslims only Ibn Karram agreed. Ibn Karram is quoted as saying Allah is 'one of essence one of substance', which the author denounces as a departure from consensus and aligning with Christians. Then the 'shubha' (sophistry) of the Christians is presented: they argue that a substance is defined as 'subsistent by itself' (qāʾim bi-dh-dhāt), and since Allah is subsistent by Himself, He would be a substance. This definition is claimed to be sound (universal and convertible). The passage ends abruptly, but the implication is that the author will refute this definition or its application to Allah.

Key Terms
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
العين (al-ʿayn)
الجسم (al-jism)
غير المتركب (ghayr al-murakkab)
ابن كرام (Ibn Karram)
شبه النصارى (shubah an-naṣārā)
قائم بالذات (qāʾim bi-dh-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can be called a substance because He subsists by Himself, and every subsistent by itself is a substance.
Correction: The definition of substance is not simply 'subsistent by itself'; substances in the witnessed world are contingent and composite, which does not apply to Allah. The passage implies that this definition is flawed or its application to Allah is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among Muslims agreed with the Christians that Allah is a substance?
A) Al-Ashʿarī
B) Ibn Karram
C) Al-Māturīdī
D) No one

Key Takeaway
Describing Allah as a substance is impossible because substances are contingent and composite, while Allah is eternal and non-composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as having shape, location, or structure—like a substance? What does this lesson reveal about the hidden assumptions you might be carrying about who Allah is? Will you actively train your mind to affirm that He is not like any created thing, even intellectually?
?
The Christians and Ibn Karram used a definition that fit created things and applied it to Allah. Are there any words or attributes you use to describe Allah that you have not purified from human limitations? What step will you take today to guard your tongue and heart from likening Allah to His creation?
?
This passage shows that even well-meaning theological reasoning can lead to grave error if it does not submit to divine transcendence. Have you ever felt confident in your own understanding of Allah, only to realize later that you were overstepping? How can you cultivate humility in your theology and constantly return to the principle of 'laysa kamithlihi shay' (nothing is like Him)?

Why God Is Not a Substance
Refutation of the argument that God is a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God must be a substance because He exists and is not an accident is invalid, based on the correct definitions of substance and accident in kalam.

Core Idea
Being a substance is not about 'not accident'—it's about being an origin of bodies.

Some theologians have argued that since everything in the world is either substance or accident, and God is not an accident, He must be a substance. This argument might seem logical, but it relies on a misunderstanding of what 'substance' actually means. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi exposes the fallacy by showing that substance and accident are defined by properties that do not apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian argument
Begin by reading the first part of the passage where the argument is presented: everything in the witnessed world is either substance or accident; God exists and is not an accident; therefore God is a substance. Understand the logical structure.
This is a syllogism that appears valid at first glance.
2
Notice the author's counter-definition
The author rejects the premise that substance is defined merely as 'not accident'. Instead, he states that substance is defined as 'an origin from which the body is composed'. Pay close attention to this redefinition.
This shift in definition is the key to the refutation.
3
Apply the definition to God
Since God cannot be an origin of a body (because He is not composed of parts and does not enter into composition), He cannot be classified as a substance according to this correct definition.
The argument fails because the classification does not transfer to God.
4
Examine the parallel refutation about accident
The author also shows that the definition of accident is not merely 'existent' but rather 'that which cannot endure'. God is necessarily enduring, so He is not an accident either.
This completes the refutation of the false dichotomy.
5
Conclude the lesson
Understand that the categories of substance and accident are tied to physical composition and impermanence. Since God transcends these, the argument that He must be a substance is invalid.
The lesson highlights the importance of precise definitions in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الموجود فى الشاهد، إما أن يكون عرضا وإما أن يكون جوهرا والله تعالى موجود وليس بعرض فيكون جوهرا كلام فاسد، لأن الجوهر في الشاهد ما كان جوهرا لأنه ليس بعرض، بل كان جوهرا لأنه أصل يتركب منه الجسم، ولهذا لم يكن العرض جوهرا لأنه ليس بأصل يتركب منه الجسم، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه كونه أصلا يتركب منه الجسم فلم يكن جوهرا، وليس من ضرورة كونه موجودا كونه جوهرا، لأن العرض موجود وليس بجوهر، وكذا العرض لم يكن عرضا، لأنه موجود لأن الجوهر موجود وليس بعرض، بل كان عرضا لاستحالة دوامه والله تعالى لا يستحيل عليه الدوام، بل هو واجب البقاء فلم يكن عرضا ولهذا لم يكن الجوهر عرضا لأنه ليس بمستحيل البقاء.
And their statement that 'the existent in the witnessed world is either an accident or a substance, and God exists and is not an accident, so He is a substance' is corrupt speech. Because the substance in the witnessed world is a substance not because it is not an accident, but rather it is a substance because it is an origin from which the body is composed. For this reason, the accident is not a substance because it is not an origin from which the body is composed. And God, may He be exalted, it is impossible for Him to be an origin from which the body is composed, so He is not a substance. And it is not necessary that because He exists, He is a substance, because the accident exists and is not a substance. Likewise, the accident is not an accident because it exists, because the substance exists and is not an accident; rather, it is an accident due to the impossibility of its enduring. And God does not have the impossibility of endurance; rather, He is necessary in permanence, so He is not an accident. For this reason, the substance is not an accident because it is not impossible to endure.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument often used by Christians (or others) to prove that God is a substance. The argument says: Everything in the witnessed world is either substance or accident. God exists and is not an accident, therefore He is a substance. The author points out the fallacy: the definition of substance in the witnessed world is not merely 'not accident' but rather 'an origin that composes the body'. God cannot be an origin of a body, so the classification fails. Moreover, existence alone does not imply substance; accidents also exist but are not substances. Similarly, the definition of accident is based on impossibility of endurance, not on existence alone. God is permanent, so He cannot be an accident. Thus the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
أصل
جسم
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God exists and is not an accident, He must be a substance.
Correction: The author shows that substance is defined by being an origin of bodies, not by not being an accident. God cannot be an origin of bodies, so He is not a substance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it invalid to conclude that God is a substance because He exists and is not an accident?
A) Because God does not exist in the witnessed world.
B) Because the definition of substance is not 'not accident' but 'origin of the body'.
C) Because God is not an accident but also not a substance.
D) Because the argument is used by Christians and thus false.

Key Takeaway
The argument that God must be a substance fails because substance and accident are defined by specific properties (origin of body and impossibility of endurance) that do not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's nature, do you catch yourself unconsciously fitting Him into categories that belong to created things—like assuming He must 'exist' in the same way you exist? What would it mean for you to let go of that comfortable but false assumption, starting right now?
?
This lesson shows that 'substance' and 'accident' are not neutral labels; they belong to the world of change and composition. Are you willing to admit that your mind sometimes tries to pin God down with familiar words, and to repent of that intellectual pride today?
?
Look at your life: have you ever treated God as if He were a 'part' of your world—something you can contain in a concept or a feeling? How would you change your worship if you truly believed He is beyond all categories? Make one concrete resolution before you close this lesson.

The Argument that God is a Substance Because He is a Doer
The author begins to refute the argument that God is a substance because He is a doer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God is a substance because He is a doer, and why the author begins to refute it by questioning the analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
An argument from analogy only works if the analogy holds.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents an objection from those who claim that since God is a doer (فاعل), and every doer in the witnessed world is a substance (جوهر), then God must also be a substance. The author begins his reply but the text cuts off. Nevertheless, this fragment teaches us about the structure of such arguments and the importance of examining whether analogies from creation apply to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the fragment
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text cuts off in the middle of the author's reply.
This fragment is only the beginning of an argument and its rebuttal.
2
Identify the analogy
The opponents draw an analogy from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (ghaib). In the witnessed world, every doer is a substance; therefore, God, as a doer, must be a substance.
This is a classic example of qiyas (analogical reasoning).
3
Observe the author's response start
The author says 'We say: And every doer in...' but does not finish. This indicates he is about to challenge the analogy, likely by pointing out differences between created doers and the Creator.
The incomplete sentence leaves the exact refutation unknown.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Consider why the author might cut off here. In the original text, the argument may continue on the next page. This teaches us to be careful when reading fragments of theological debates.
Always consider context and completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الله تعالى فاعل وكل فاعل في الشاهد جوهر . قلنا : وكل فاعل في
And their statement: 'God is a doer, and every doer in the witnessed world is a substance.' We say: 'And every doer in...'
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of another argument: opponents claim that because God is a doer (فاعل), and every doer in the witnessed world is a substance, then God must be a substance. The author begins his reply with 'We say: And every doer in...' but the text cuts off at this point. The argument is incomplete in the available pages. We can infer that the author would likely reject the analogy, but the full response is not given here.

Key Terms

جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is complete or that the author agrees with the objection.
Correction: The text cuts off, so the full reply is not given. However, from Maturidi principles, the author would reject the analogy. The argument is incomplete and should not be taken as a valid proof.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the argument that God is a substance because He is a doer?
A) Every doer in the witnessed world is a substance.
B) God is not an accident.
C) God creates substances.
D) The doer must be a body.

Key Takeaway
This fragment shows how some attempted to prove God is a substance by analogy, but the author begins to refute, reminding us that analogies from creation do not automatically apply to the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to bring that assumption before the Quran and sound reasoning today, even if it means letting go of a comfortable idea?

The Corporealist Argument from Analogy and Tripartite Division
Presents the corporealist argument via analogy from the witnessed to the unseen and tripartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments used by corporealists (mujassimah) to claim that God must be a body.

Core Idea
If everything seen is a body, the unseen must also be a body.

This passage presents the view of those who argue that God is a body. They rely on two key arguments: first, an analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen; second, a classification of all existents into three categories that leaves no room for a non-corporeal God. Understanding this argument is essential to grasping the mainstream Sunni refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first argument
Read the first part of the passage: 'الشاهد جسم... فكذا ها هنا'. Notice how the author uses analogy (qiyas) from the witnessed to the unseen.
2
Examine the appeal to intuition
The passage then claims that common sense immediately assumes any living, hearing, seeing being is a body. This is used as a 'necessary truth'.
The author says even common people would not doubt it.
3
Understand the tripartite division
The second argument states that all existents are either accidents, substances, or bodies. Since God cannot be an accident or substance, He must be a body.
This classification is presented as exhaustive.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The passage ends by warning that if God is removed from all three categories, He would be non-existent, so saying He is not a body is equivalent to atheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشاهد جسم، ويستحيل اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الأوصاف، وما يستحيل في الشاهد يستحيل في الغائب ألا ترى أنه كما يستحيل في الشاهد أن يكون ما ليس عالما قادرا فاعلا، يستحيل مثله في الغائب فكذا ها هنا كما يستحيل ثبوت هذه الأوصاف لما ليس بجسم في الشاهد يستحيل في الغائب، على أنه تقرر في بداية عقول العوام استحالة اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الصفات، حتى إن مخبرا لو أخبر أنه رأى حيا سمعيا بصيرا، لم يشك أحد أنه كان جسما، ولا يشتغل بالسؤال أنه كان جسما أو غير جسم، استغناء منه بما علم ببديهة عقله بكونه جسما، وما هذا سبيله ليس بإثبات مثله ممكنا لا في الشاهد ولا في الغائب، ولأنه تعالى ليس بعرض ولا جوهر لما مر، فلو لم يكن جسما لالتحق بالعدم لانحصار الموجودات على هذه الأقسام الثلاثة أعني أنها أعراض وجواهر وأجسام ولا يعقل موجود خارج عن هذه الأقسام، فلو خرج عن كونه جسما مع خروجه عن كونه عرضا وجوهرا لم يبق إلى إثباته طريق، فكان القول بإخراجه عن هذه الأقسام قولا بعدمه.
The witnessed is a body, and it is impossible for what is not a body to be described with these attributes, and what is impossible in the witnessed is impossible in the unseen. Do you not see that just as it is impossible in the witnessed for what is not knowing, able, acting, the like of it is impossible in the unseen. So likewise here, just as it is impossible to establish these attributes for what is not a body in the witnessed, it is impossible in the unseen. Moreover, it has been established in the beginnings of the intellects of the common people the impossibility of describing what is not a body with these attributes, such that if a reporter reported that he saw a living, hearing, seeing being, no one would doubt that it was a body, nor would he bother to ask whether it was a body or not, relying on his intuitive knowledge that it is a body. And what is like this is not possible to establish its like, neither in the witnessed nor in the unseen. And because the Exalted is neither an accident nor a substance, as has passed, so if He were not a body, He would be attached to non-existence due to the confinement of existents to these three categories, namely: they are accidents, substances, and bodies, and no existent is conceivable outside these categories. So if He were to exit from being a body, along with exiting from being an accident and substance, there would remain no way to prove Him, so the statement of removing Him from these categories is a statement of His non-existence.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for corporealism (tajsim), likely from the perspective of an opponent such as the Karramiyya or anthropomorphists. It begins with a basic premise: the witnessed world (shahid) consists of bodies. The attributes attributed to God—life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight—are only found in bodies in the witnessed world. By the principle of analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), what is impossible in the witnessed is impossible in the unseen. Therefore, God must be a body. The author then appeals to common intuition: if someone reports seeing a living, hearing, seeing being, everyone immediately assumes it is a body without question. This is said to be a necessary truth of the intellect. The second argument uses the tripartite classification of all existents into accidents (a'rad), substances (jawāhir), and bodies (ajsām). Since God cannot be an accident (because accidents are dependent on bodies) nor a substance (because substances are also dependent on bodies or are themselves bodies), the only remaining category is body. If He is excluded from all three, He would be non-existent. Thus, the corporealist concludes that God must be a body. This is a presentation of the view that will be refuted in the following lessons.

Key Terms
جسم
شاهد
غائب
عرض
جوهر
أعراض
أجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage presents the author's own position that God is a body.
Correction: The passage actually presents the argument of the corporealist opponents. The author is about to refute this view in the following lessons.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the corporealist argument in this passage?
A) The witnessed is a body.
B) God is an accident.
C) The unseen is unlike the witnessed.
D) Bodies are infinite.

Key Takeaway
The corporealist argument relies on an analogy from the observed world and a tripartite classification of beings to conclude that God must be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you unconsciously imagine Him with human-like qualities? How does this passage's flawed analogy expose the danger of reducing Allah to what your mind can grasp? What will you do to combat this tendency?
?
The tripartite classification claims to be exhaustive—but what assumptions about reality are you holding that might limit your understanding of Allah? Are you willing to accept that He is beyond all categories you know?
?
The corporealist argument relies on 'common intuition.' Can you identify an area in your own faith where you have accepted something about Allah based on intuition rather than revelation? How will you re-calibrate your thinking to align with the Qur'an and Sunnah?

Corporealism Leads to Absurdities
Sanusi presents the core reductio of corporealism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main absurd consequences that the people of truth argue follow from claiming Allah is a body.

Core Idea
Corporealism logically forces absurdities about Allah and the world.

The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) begin their refutation of corporealism by showing that it leads to multiple impossible conclusions. They do not directly disprove corporealism but demonstrate that any belief in a corporeal God inevitably entails statements that are clearly false, thereby proving corporealism false itself. This lesson presents that overall claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the strong declarative style: the author states the consequences as certain.
2
Identify the three consequences
The passage lists three absurd outcomes of corporealism. Find them and phrase them in your own words.
The first consequence is actually a disjunction: either the world is eternal or God is temporal.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider how each consequence is truly absurd. For example, if God is temporal, then He would be created, which contradicts His being the Creator.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي أهل الحق في التجسيم: فأما أهل الحق فأنهم يقولون بأن القول بأن الله تعالى جسم مؤتلف متبعض متجزئ يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم أو القول بحدوث الباري جل وعلا، والقول بعدم الصانع للعالم، ويؤدي أيضا إلى إبطال دليل التوحيد، وكل ذلك باطل؛ فكان القول بالتجسيم باطلا. أما الأول وهو أنه يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم أو حدوث الصانع، فتقريره من وجهين أحدهما أنه تعالى لو كان جسما مؤتلفا له أبعاض وأجزاء. إما أن كان متناهيا، وإما أن لم يكن متناهيا، ولا وجه إلى القول بعدم التناهي لأن كل جزء منه متناهي الذات، وخروج ما اجتمع من الأجزاء المتناهية عن المتناهي في الذات محال، ولو جاز ذا لجاز كون العالم غير متناه في جميع الجهات كما يزعم أهل الدهر وذلك باطل، ولأنه لابد من القول بالتناهي إذا وجد العالم لا في ذاته فبقى هو بذاته من العالم بجهة من الجهات ضرورة استحالة كون الباري جل وعلا مداخلا أجزاء العالم، وإذا ثبت تناهيه ثبت كونه على قدر مخصوص جميع مع كون غيره من الأقدار مساويا له في الجواز، واختصاص أحد الجائزين لن يثبت إلا بمخصص، إذ لا مزية له بذاته على ما يساويه في الجواز
The opinion of the people of truth on corporealism: As for the people of truth, they say that the statement that Allah the Exalted is a composite body, with parts and divisions, leads to the statement of the eternity of the world or the statement of the temporality of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, and the statement of the absence of the Creator of the world, and also leads to invalidating the proof of oneness, and all of that is false. So the statement of corporealism is false. As for the first [consequence], namely that it leads to the statement of the eternity of the world or the temporality of the Creator, its elaboration is from two aspects: first, that if He were a composite body having parts and limbs, either He is finite or He is not finite. There is no way to say non-finiteness because every part of Him is finite in essence, and for the aggregate of finite parts to exit from finiteness in essence is impossible. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible for the world to be infinite in all directions, as the Materialists claim, and that is false. And because it is necessary to say finiteness when the world exists not in His essence, so He remains by His essence from the world by some direction, due to the impossibility of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, being interpenetrating with the parts of the world. And when His finiteness is established, it is established that He is of a specific measure along with the fact that other measures are equal to Him in permissibility, and the specification of one of the permissible [measures] cannot be established except by a specifier, since there is no superiority in His essence over what equals it in permissibility.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of corporealism by the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq). It opens with a heading declaring the true position. The author first states that the claim of corporealism leads to three disastrous consequences: (1) either the world is eternal (qidam al-‘ālam) or God is temporal (ḥudūth al-Bārī), (2) the denial of a Creator, (3) nullification of the proof of divine oneness (tawḥīd). All are false, so corporealism is false. The author then elaborates the first consequence in two aspects (wajhan). The first aspect argues that if God is a composite body with parts, He must be either finite or infinite. Finiteness is necessary because each part is finite, and an aggregate of finite parts cannot be infinite. If infinite were possible, the world could be infinite, as the Materialists (Ahl al-Dahr) claim, which is false. Moreover, since the world exists outside God, God must be spatially related to it, which implies finitude. Once finiteness is established, God must have a specific size. But other sizes are equally possible; there is no inherent reason for one size over another. Thus, a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is needed to choose that particular size, leading to a regress or external creator—which defeats the purpose of affirming God.

جسم مؤتلف
قدم العالم

Corporealism is false because it necessarily leads to three absurd conclusions.

The Finite Body Dilemma
Sanusi details the first absurd consequence via a finite/infinite dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first detailed argument showing that a corporeal God must be finite and thus require a specifier, which is impossible.

Core Idea
A composite body cannot be infinite; finiteness forces a need for a specifier.

Having established that corporealism leads to absurdities, the author now elaborates the first consequence—that corporealism implies either the world is eternal or God is temporal. The first aspect of this elaboration argues that if God is a composite body, He must be finite. However, finiteness creates a new problem: why does He have a specific size rather than any other? This demands an external specifier, which contradicts His being the ultimate Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if He were a composite body... then...'
This is a proof by contradiction.
2
Follow the dilemma
Identify the two options: finite or infinite. The passage argues that infinite is impossible, so He must be finite.
The argument uses the impossibility of an infinite aggregate of finite parts.
3
Understand the specifier conclusion
Once finite, why a specific size? The passage says that without a specifier, there is no reason for one size over another, so it requires an external cause.
This causes a regress, making God caused.
