A Body Needs a Shape, a Shape Needs a Specifier
Second refutation: body requires shape, shape requires specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a physical body to Allah leads to the necessity of a specifier for its shape, which is incompatible with His self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
A body needs a shape; a shape needs a specifier; for Allah, that is impossible.

This lesson presents the second aspect of the first consequence in refuting corporealism. If Allah were a body, He would have specific dimensions and a particular shape. But any specific shape needs a cause that selects it over other equally possible shapes. This leads to an infinite regress or a creator other than Allah, both impossible. The passage builds a logical case that a composite body cannot be self-sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument proceeds: body → dimensions → shape → specifier.
The text ends mid-sentence but the conclusion is clear.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify each step: 1) A composite body has three dimensions. 2) It must have a specific shape (round, square, etc.). 3) It cannot have all shapes simultaneously because that would be contradictory. 4) Therefore it has one shape. 5) There is no reason for that shape over others, so a specifier is needed.
3
Understand the parity argument
The author argues that if it were permissible for God to have all shapes in the unseen while impossible in the witnessed, then contradictory attributes (motion/rest, black/white) could also coexist in God. This is absurd.
This shows that the same logical rules apply to the unseen and witnessed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since a specifier is required, either we have an infinite regress or another being specified Allah. Both are impossible for the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتقرير الثاني - أن ما كان متبعضا متجزئا لابد من أن يكون طويلا أو عريضا أو طويلا عريضا وهو السطح أو طويلا عريضا عميقا وهو المكعب، ثم إذا كان كذلك، لابد من أن يكون مدورا أو مثلثا أو مربعا أو مخمسا أو مسدسا أو مسبعا أو مثمنا أو غير ذلك مما يطول ذكره، فبعد ذلك إما أن يكون على هذه الأشكال أجمع فيكون مدورا مثلثا مربعا إلخ ذلك، وإما أن يكون على شكل من هذه الأشكال (ولا) جائز أن يكون على الأشكال كلها لما فيها من التناهي والتضاد، ولو جاز ذا في الغائب مع امتناعه في الشاهد لجاز في الغائب اجتماع الحركة والسكون والسواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق وحيث يبطل (هذا) بطل الأول لاستوائهما في الاستحالة في الشاهد فلم يبق إلا أنه على شكل من هذه الأشكال، وهيئة من هذه الهيئات، ولا مزية لما اختص به على غيره بما يساويه من الأشكال في الجواز، فبعد ذلك إما أن يقال إنما اختص بالثبوت من الأشكال الأخرى اختص لا بتخصيص مخصص،
And the second elaboration: That which is divided and partitioned must be long, or wide, or long and wide (which is a surface), or long, wide, and deep (which is a cube). Then when it is like that, it must be round, or triangular, or square, or pentagonal, or hexagonal, or heptagonal, or octagonal, or other than that which would take long to mention. Then after that, either it is upon all these shapes, so it would be round, triangular, square, etc., or upon one of these shapes. It is not permissible for it to be upon all shapes due to what they contain of finiteness and opposition. And if this were permissible in the unseen while it is impossible in the witnessed, then it would be permissible in the unseen for motion and rest, blackness and whiteness, conjunction and separation to coexist. And when this is invalidated, the first is invalidated due to their equal status in impossibility in the witnessed. So nothing remains except that it is upon one of these shapes and one of these forms. And there is no superiority for what it is specified with over what equals it from the shapes in permissibility. Then after that, either it is said that it was specified for existence over the other shapes by a specification without a specifier...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect (al-taqrīr al-thānī) of the first consequence in the refutation of corporealism. The argument proceeds as follows: any composite body (mutaba‘‘iḍ mutajazzi’) must have three dimensions: length, width, and depth. It must therefore have a specific geometric shape (e.g., round, triangular, square, etc.). It cannot have all shapes simultaneously because that would involve finiteness and contradiction. If it were possible for God to have all shapes in the unseen while it is impossible in the witnessed, then by parity of reasoning, contradictory attributes like motion and rest, black and white, could also coexist in God—which is absurd. Therefore, God must have one specific shape. But there is no inherent reason for one shape over another; all shapes are equally possible. Thus, a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is needed to choose that shape. This leads either to an infinite regress or to a creator other than God, which is impossible. The text ends here, but the conclusion is clear: the need for a specifier implies that God is not self-sufficient or that another being must have specified Him, both of which are unacceptable.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shapes are merely physical properties and irrelevant to the discussion of God's nature.
Correction: The argument shows that if Allah were a body, He would necessarily have a specific shape, and that shape would require a cause. This demonstrates that corporealism leads to an infinite regress or dependence on another being, contradicting His necessity.

The Corporealism Argument: Absurd Consequences
Nasafi refutes corporealism by deducing its impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirming that God is a body leads to the absurd conclusions of either the pre-eternity of the world or the origination of God.

Core Idea
Saying God is a body forces you to deny creation or accept a created God.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against corporealism. He shows that if God were a body, the world could also be eternal, or God would have to be originated—both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi begins by stating a conditional: if being a body were permissible for God, it would be permissible for the world, undermining the proof of the world's origination.
This is a common kalam strategy: showing that an opponent's view leads to rejecting their own accepted proofs.
2
Follow the second possibility
The passage then presents a second possibility: that God's being a body is due to a specifier. Nasafi argues that this would make God originated and subject to another's power, leading to an infinite regress—already shown invalid.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered impossible in kalam.
3
Recognize the final conclusion
The passage concludes that corporealism leads to either the pre-eternity of the world (if body is inherent) or the origination of God (if specified). Both are impossible, so corporealism is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assuming the proposition leads to contradiction, so the proposition is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز ذا لجاز في العالم، ولم يثبت دليل حدوثه، وافتقاره إلى صانع أوجده، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على إبطاله، وخصماؤنا (۱) في هذه المسألة ساعدونا على ذلك. وإما أن يقال بأنه اختص بذلك بتخصيص مخصص. ولو كان كذلك لكان محدثا محلا لقبول قدرة الغير وهو محال، إذ لو كان محدثا، لكان له محدث وكذا الكلام في الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا يتناهى والقول ببطلان ذلك قد مر، فثبت أن القول بكونه جسما يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم وتعطيل الصانع، أو إلى القول بحدوث الصانع وذلك محال وكل قول يؤدي إلى المحال فهو محال - ولا يقال إن عندكم اختص بكونه حيا عالما قادرا سميعا بصيرا، ولا يقال إن اختصاصه كان بتخصيص مخصص وكذا هذا، لأنا نقول : اختصاصه بأحد الجائزين لن يكون إلا بتخصص مخصص، وهذه الصفات واجبة الوجود لا جائزة الوجود، وأضدادها ممتنعة الوجود لما أنها نقائص، ويستحيل ثبوت النقص على القديم، ونحن ندعي استحالة اختصاص أحد(۲) الجائزين، أو أحد الوجود الجائزة المحتملة مع مساواة غيره إياه في الجواز، وانعدام دليل المزية، وما ألزموه ليس من هذا القبيل، فأما ما أثبتوه فهو من هذا القبيل، لأنه ليس في شكل من هذه الأشكال كما لا يساويه غيره فيه، ولا نقيضه ولا يساويه(۳) فيها غيره من الأشكال، فثبت الاستواء في ذلك، وفيما ألزموا الأمر بخلافه حتى إن من المشاهد لما كانت النقائص جائزة على كل محدث لم تثبت صفة من هذه الصفات التي صفات الكمال إلا بتخصيص مخصص فكذا هذا، والذي يحقق استواء هذه الأشكال في الجواز أن من عين واحدا منها لم ينفصل ممن عين آخر، ولا دليل على صحة ما ادعاه وإبطال ما ادعى خصمه، يحققه أنه ليس في بعضها من المدح أو من النقيصة ما هي ليس فيها وراءه، وفي صفات الكمال الأمر بخلافه وبالله التوفيق.
And if this were permissible, it would be permissible in the world, and the proof of its temporal origination and its need for a Maker who brought it into existence would not be established, and we have already established the proof of its invalidity, and our opponents in this issue have assisted us in that. Or it might be said that it was distinguished by the specification of a specifier. And if that were the case, it would be originated, a locus for receiving the power of another, and that is impossible, for if it were originated, it would have an originator, and likewise the speech about the second and third ad infinitum, and the statement of the invalidity of that has already passed. So it is established that the statement that He is a body leads to the statement of the pre-eternity of the world and the suspension of the Maker, or to the statement of the temporal origination of the Maker, and that is impossible, and every statement that leads to the impossible is impossible. And it cannot be said: 'In your view, He is distinguished by being living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, and it cannot be said that His distinction was by the specification of a specifier, and likewise this.' For we say: His distinction to one of the two possible things cannot be except by the specification of a specifier, and these attributes are necessary of existence, not possible of existence, and their opposites are impossible of existence because they are deficiencies, and it is impossible for deficiency to be established for the Eternal. And we claim the impossibility of the distinction of one of the two possible things, or one of the possible contingent existences, with the equality of others to it in possibility, and the absence of evidence of superiority, and what they have imposed is not of this category. As for what they have affirmed, it is of this category, because there is no form among these forms such that nothing else equals it in it, nor its opposite nor does anything else equal it in them among the forms. So equality is established in that, and in what they have imposed the matter is contrary to it, such that from observation, when deficiencies are possible for every originated being, no attribute of these attributes of perfection is established except by the specification of a specifier, so likewise this. And what confirms the equality of these forms in possibility is that whoever specifies one of them is not distinct from whoever specifies another, and there is no evidence for the validity of his claim and the invalidity of what his opponent claimed. It confirms that there is not in some of them any praise or deficiency that is not in the other, and in the attributes of perfection the matter is contrary to that. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a sustained rational proof refuting the claim that God is a body (jism). The author presents an argument by showing the absurd consequences of corporealism. He begins by stating that if being a body were permissible for God, it would be permissible for the world as well, thus undermining the proof of the world's temporal origination (ḥudūth) and its need for a Maker. Since the opponents of the Mutakallimūn themselves accept these proofs, they are inconsistent if they affirm corporealism. Next, the author considers the possibility that God's being a body is due to a specific cause (takhṣīṣ muṣakhkhiṣ). He argues that if God were specified to be a body, He would be originated (muḥdath) and a locus for the power of another, which is impossible. This leads to an infinite regress, already shown to be invalid. Thus, saying God is a body leads either to the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-‘ālam) and suspension of the Maker (ta‘ṭīl al-Ṣāni‘), or to the origination of the Maker (ḥudūth al-Ṣāni‘) — both absurd. The author then addresses an objection: why not say God's being living, knowing, etc. is also a specification without a cause? He responds by distinguishing these attributes: they are necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), not possible (jā’iz al-wujūd); their opposites are impossible because they are deficiencies. The Eternal cannot have deficiencies. In contrast, being a body is a possible attribute with no inherent superiority over its opposite. The author emphasizes that in possible matters, without a distinguishing factor (muzīyya), no one option can be claimed over another. The objection's case is different because divine attributes of perfection are necessary, not possible. Thus, the argument against corporealism stands.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming God as a body is a valid interpretation of scripture and does not lead to logical problems.
Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that corporealism necessarily leads to one of two absurdities: either the world is eternal (denying creation) or God is originated (making Him like creation). Both contradict fundamental Islamic teachings and rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi in this passage, what two absurd consequences does affirming that God is a body lead to?
A) The suspension of prophecy and the denial of angels
B) The pre-eternity of the world and the origination of God
C) The denial of divine attributes and the affirmation of polytheism
D) The impossibility of miracles and the denial of resurrection

Key Takeaway
Corporealism is impossible because it entails either denying the creation of the world or making God originated—both rationally absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific thought or image do you need to reject, and how will you actively purify your concept of Allah starting today?
?
The argument relies on the certainty that the world is created and dependent on Allah every moment. Do you truly live as if you and everything around you are completely dependent on Him for existence at every instant? Identify one area—your health, your wealth, your relationships—where you act as if it runs independently. What will you do today to consciously acknowledge Allah's constant creation in that area?
?
The passage ends with 'every statement that leads to the impossible is impossible.' Are you holding onto any belief about Allah or religion that, if you followed it to its logical end, would lead to an impossibility? Be honest: what is that belief, and will you have the courage to abandon it for the sake of sound tawhid?

Necessary vs. Possible: The Response to the Objection
Nasafi distinguishes necessary divine attributes from possible attributes to counter an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of perfection do not require a specifier, unlike the attribute of being a body.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are necessary, not possible—they do not require an external specifier.

An opponent might argue that if God's being a body requires a specifier, then His being living, knowing, etc. also requires one. Nasafi responds by distinguishing necessary attributes from possible ones. He explains that the attributes of perfection are necessary for God, while being a body is merely possible and would need a cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the beginning of the passage where the opponent says: 'In your view, He is distinguished by being living, knowing, powerful... and it cannot be said that His distinction was by the specification of a specifier, and likewise this.' Recognize the challenge.
The opponent is trying to use the same logic against the Kalam position.
2
Grasp the key distinction
Nasafi responds by stating that the attributes of perfection are necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), not possible (jā’iz al-wujūd). Their opposites are deficiencies, which cannot apply to the Eternal. Therefore, these attributes do not need a specifier; they are inherent to God's essence.
This distinction is crucial in Kalam: necessary attributes belong to God by essence, not by external cause.
3
Apply the distinction to the equality argument
Nasafi further explains that in possible matters (like being a body or not), all options are equal unless there is a distinguishing factor. Since no such factor exists for God being a body, it cannot be affirmed. But for the attributes of perfection, the opposite is impossible, so they are necessarily affirmed.
Thus, the objection fails because it conflates necessary and possible attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز ذا لجاز في العالم، ولم يثبت دليل حدوثه، وافتقاره إلى صانع أوجده، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على إبطاله، وخصماؤنا (۱) في هذه المسألة ساعدونا على ذلك. وإما أن يقال بأنه اختص بذلك بتخصيص مخصص. ولو كان كذلك لكان محدثا محلا لقبول قدرة الغير وهو محال، إذ لو كان محدثا، لكان له محدث وكذا الكلام في الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا يتناهى والقول ببطلان ذلك قد مر، فثبت أن القول بكونه جسما يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم وتعطيل الصانع، أو إلى القول بحدوث الصانع وذلك محال وكل قول يؤدي إلى المحال فهو محال - ولا يقال إن عندكم اختص بكونه حيا عالما قادرا سميعا بصيرا، ولا يقال إن اختصاصه كان بتخصيص مخصص وكذا هذا، لأنا نقول : اختصاصه بأحد الجائزين لن يكون إلا بتخصص مخصص، وهذه الصفات واجبة الوجود لا جائزة الوجود، وأضدادها ممتنعة الوجود لما أنها نقائص، ويستحيل ثبوت النقص على القديم، ونحن ندعي استحالة اختصاص أحد(۲) الجائزين، أو أحد الوجود الجائزة المحتملة مع مساواة غيره إياه في الجواز، وانعدام دليل المزية، وما ألزموه ليس من هذا القبيل، فأما ما أثبتوه فهو من هذا القبيل، لأنه ليس في شكل من هذه الأشكال كما لا يساويه غيره فيه، ولا نقيضه ولا يساويه(۳) فيها غيره من الأشكال، فثبت الاستواء في ذلك، وفيما ألزموا الأمر بخلافه حتى إن من المشاهد لما كانت النقائص جائزة على كل محدث لم تثبت صفة من هذه الصفات التي صفات الكمال إلا بتخصيص مخصص فكذا هذا، والذي يحقق استواء هذه الأشكال في الجواز أن من عين واحدا منها لم ينفصل ممن عين آخر، ولا دليل على صحة ما ادعاه وإبطال ما ادعى خصمه، يحققه أنه ليس في بعضها من المدح أو من النقيصة ما هي ليس فيها وراءه، وفي صفات الكمال الأمر بخلافه وبالله التوفيق.
And if this were permissible, it would be permissible in the world, and the proof of its temporal origination and its need for a Maker who brought it into existence would not be established, and we have already established the proof of its invalidity, and our opponents in this issue have assisted us in that. Or it might be said that it was distinguished by the specification of a specifier. And if that were the case, it would be originated, a locus for receiving the power of another, and that is impossible, for if it were originated, it would have an originator, and likewise the speech about the second and third ad infinitum, and the statement of the invalidity of that has already passed. So it is established that the statement that He is a body leads to the statement of the pre-eternity of the world and the suspension of the Maker, or to the statement of the temporal origination of the Maker, and that is impossible, and every statement that leads to the impossible is impossible. And it cannot be said: 'In your view, He is distinguished by being living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, and it cannot be said that His distinction was by the specification of a specifier, and likewise this.' For we say: His distinction to one of the two possible things cannot be except by the specification of a specifier, and these attributes are necessary of existence, not possible of existence, and their opposites are impossible of existence because they are deficiencies, and it is impossible for deficiency to be established for the Eternal. And we claim the impossibility of the distinction of one of the two possible things, or one of the possible contingent existences, with the equality of others to it in possibility, and the absence of evidence of superiority, and what they have imposed is not of this category. As for what they have affirmed, it is of this category, because there is no form among these forms such that nothing else equals it in it, nor its opposite nor does anything else equal it in them among the forms. So equality is established in that, and in what they have imposed the matter is contrary to it, such that from observation, when deficiencies are possible for every originated being, no attribute of these attributes of perfection is established except by the specification of a specifier, so likewise this. And what confirms the equality of these forms in possibility is that whoever specifies one of them is not distinct from whoever specifies another, and there is no evidence for the validity of his claim and the invalidity of what his opponent claimed. It confirms that there is not in some of them any praise or deficiency that is not in the other, and in the attributes of perfection the matter is contrary to that. And with God is success.

Correction: Nasafi shows that the attributes of perfection are necessary, not possible. They are inherent to God's essence and do not need an external cause, unlike possible attributes such as being a body.

?

Think of one specific area—maybe a trial you face, or a prayer unanswered—where you doubted God's power or wisdom. How does the truth that these attributes are necessary and without deficiency transform your perspective? What commitment will you make to replace that doubt with certainty?

The Dilemma of Composition: Deficiencies or Polytheism
Nasafi presents a dilemma: composition of God leads to either each part being deficient (created) or each part being divine (polytheism).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God is composed leads to an impossible dilemma, undermining the doctrine of divine unity.

Core Idea
Composition forces God to be either deficient or multiplied — both contradictory to His nature.

Nasafi presents a second proof against corporealism, exposing a logical dilemma. If God were composed of parts, each part must be self-subsistent. Then either each part possesses all perfections (making it a god) or lacks them (making it originated). Neither is acceptable, so corporealism is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation together. Try to grasp the flow of the argument without over-analyzing.
The passage is a single logical argument, so reading once helps see the structure.
2
Identify the dilemma's two horns
Nasafi presents two possibilities: either each part of God possesses the attributes of perfection, or it does not. Find where these are stated.
The options are signaled by 'fa-immā an yakūna kullu juz’in minhu mawsūfan bi-ṣifāti al-kamāl... wa immā an lam yakun mawsūfan bihā'.
3
Understand the consequences of each option
Trace the implications: if a part lacks perfection, it must possess deficiency, proving it is created. If it has perfection, it becomes a god, leading to polytheism. Both are impossible for the One Eternal God.
The author argues that deficiency indicates temporality, while perfection implies lordship.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since both options lead to absurdities, the initial assumption that God is composed must be false. Nasafi reaffirms that the proofs of tawḥīd are certain, so corporealism is false.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the corporealist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني، وهو أن القول بما قالوا يؤدي إلى إبطال دلالة التوحيد فهو أنه تعالى لو كان مؤتلفا متبعضا متجزئا لكان كل جزء منه قائما بنفسه إذ الائتلاف على ما لا قيام له بذاته محال، ولأن كل جزء منه لو لم يكن له قيام بذاته لاستحال أن يكون للكل قيام بذاته، فما ذا كل ذاته لا قيام له بذاته، ووجود تخليق العالم ممن لا قيام له بذاته محال، ولو كان كل جزء منه قائما بذاته فإما أن كان كل جزء منه موصوفا بصفات الكمال كالحياة والعلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر والإرادة، وإما أن لم يكن موصوفا بها، بل كان الموصوف بها جزء واحدا منه أو بعض الأجزاء دون البعض، فإن (١) لم يكن كل جزء منها موصوفا بصفات الكمال لكان موصوفا بأضدادها من صفات النقص، كالموت والجهل والعجز والصمم والعمى والاضطرار، إذ ما كان قائما بذاته لا يستحيل عليه قبول صفات الكمال باعتبار ذاته، إذ كل قائم بالذات يجوز قبوله للصفات، وما لا يقوم به إنما لا يقوم لقيام ضده به. ولو كان موصوفا (۲) بصفات النقص لكان محدثا، فإذا(۳) ما لا يوصف من أجزائه بكونه حياة قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا فهو محدث لقيام دليل الحدوث به، وكون أجزاء (4) القديم محدثة، محال، وإن كان كل جزء منه موصوفا بصفات الكمال - - فكان كل جزء حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا لكان كل جزء منه موصوفا بصفات الربوبية، فيكون القول به قولا بآلهة كثيرة، ويلزمون بعد ذلك ما مر من دلائل التوحيد، فإن كانت تلك الدلائل صحيحة كان القول بالتجسيم باطلا، وإن كانت تلك الدلائل فاسدة، كان القول بوحدانية الصانع باطلا، وقد ثبت وحدانية الصانع بالدلائل الموجبة للعلم الثابت على طريق التيقن، فكان القول بالتجسيم باطلا.
As for the second [point], which is that the statement of what they said leads to the invalidation of the proof of divine unity, it is that if the Exalted were composed, divided into parts, fragmented, then each part of Him would be self-subsistent, because composition of that which is not self-subsistent is impossible. And because if each part of Him were not self-subsistent, it would be impossible for the whole to be self-subsistent, so what the whole of His essence is not self-subsistent, and the existence of the creation of the world from one who is not self-subsistent is impossible. And if each part of Him were self-subsistent, then either each part of Him is described by the attributes of perfection like life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, and will, or it is not described by them, but rather that which is described by them is one part of Him or some parts without others. For if (1) not every part of them is described by the attributes of perfection, then it would be described by their opposites from the attributes of deficiency, like death, ignorance, impotence, deafness, blindness, and compulsion, since that which is self-subsistent is not impossible for it to accept attributes of perfection in consideration of its essence, for every self-subsistent thing is potentially capable of accepting attributes, and that which does not accept them only does not accept them because its opposite subsists in it. And if it were described (2) by the attributes of deficiency, it would be originated. Thus (3) whatever of its parts is not described by being living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, is originated because the proof of temporal origination subsists in it, and the existence of parts of the Eternal being originated is impossible. And if each part of Him were described by the attributes of perfection, then each part would be living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, then each part of Him would be described by the attributes of lordship, so the statement of it would be a statement of many gods. And they would be bound after that by what has passed of the proofs of divine unity. For if those proofs are sound, then the statement of corporealism is false; and if those proofs are corrupt, then the statement of the oneness of the Maker is false. And the oneness of the Maker has been established by proofs that necessitate certain knowledge on the path of certainty, so the statement of corporealism is false.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a second proof (al-thānī) against corporealism, specifically focusing on the impossibility of God being composed (mu’talf, mutaba‘‘iḍ, mutajazzi’). The author structures the argument as a dilemma. First, if God is composed, each part must be self-subsistent (qā’im bi-dhātihi), because composition of something not self-subsistent is impossible. Moreover, if the parts were not self-subsistent, the whole could not be self-subsistent, and a non-self-subsistent being cannot create the world. So each part must be self-subsistent. Then the author proposes a disjunction: either every part is described by the attributes of perfection (life, knowledge, power, etc.) or not. If not, then the parts lacking these attributes must be described by their opposites — the attributes of deficiency (death, ignorance, etc.). Since a self-subsistent entity is capable of receiving attributes, any part that lacks perfection must have deficiency, and deficiency indicates temporal origination (ḥudūth). Thus those parts are originated, which is impossible for the Eternal. If, on the other hand, every part is described by the attributes of perfection (life, knowledge, etc.), then each part would possess the attributes of lordship (rubūbiyya), leading to a belief in many gods (āliha kathīra). This contradicts the established proofs of divine unity (tawḥīd). The author concludes by stating that since the proofs of God's oneness are sound and certain, the claim of corporealism is necessarily false.

Key Terms
تأليف
تبعض
تجزؤ
قيام بالذات
صفات الكمال
صفات النقص
الربوبية
تجسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if we assume atomism or that composition means physical parts.
Correction: The argument is purely logical: any composition (whether physical or conceptual) requires parts that are self-subsistent. The dilemma applies regardless of the nature of the parts.

Checkpoint
What is the second possibility in the dilemma on composition, and what does it entail?
A) That each part lacks perfection, so it is eternal
B) That each part is described by perfection, leading to many gods
C) That only one part has perfection, so God is one
D) That no part has any attributes, so God is simple

Key Takeaway
Composition of God is impossible because it leads to either deficiency (createdness) or multiplicity (polytheism), both of which contradict His oneness and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined Allah as having any kind of 'parts'—even in the way you think about His attributes as separate? How does this dilemma force you to purify your concept of tawḥīd, and what specific step will you take today to ensure your heart never harbors such a thought?
?
When you look at your own life, do you sometimes treat one of Allah's attributes as if it were isolated from the others—for example, trusting His mercy while forgetting His justice? How does the argument that each perfect attribute belongs to the same indivisible Lord change the way you should approach Him?
?
The passage says the proofs of divine unity are 'certain.' Are you as certain of tawḥīd as this demands? What doubt—even a small one—lurks in your heart, and how will you use this logical argument to uproot it and replace it with unwavering conviction?

Refuting the Claim that God Must Be a Body
Nasafi refutes the claim that God must be a body because existents are limited to three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that God must be a body because all existents are either substance, accident, or body.

Core Idea
You cannot force God into human categories without contradiction.

Nasafi confronts a sophistical argument that tries to limit God's existence to the categories of body, substance, or accident. He shows that this classification is not exhaustive and leads to absurdities when applied to the Creator. The passage uses a powerful parity argument with Christian theology and then grounds the refutation in the principle that marks of origination cannot be applied to the Eternal.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the opening lines of the passage to understand the sophism: if God is not an accident or substance, He must be a body or else non-existent. Note the underlying assumption that all existents fall into these three categories.
This objection was raised by some theologians who tried to force God into a category.
2
Follow the parity argument
Nasafi first responds by asking how the opponents can distinguish themselves from Christians who make a parallel claim. Pay attention to the back-and-forth: the opponents argue for body, Christians for substance. Nasafi shows both are equally invalid because both entail marks of origination.
The parity argument exposes the inconsistency in the opponents' reasoning.
3
Grasp the foundational principle
Nasafi then uncovers the real method: God's existence is proven by necessary proofs, and His freedom from marks of origination is also known. Affirming marks of origination would be like affirming the world's eternity while it has marks of origination — absurd.
This principle is key: whatever indicates contingency cannot be true of the Necessary Being.
4
Understand the conclusion
Finally, Nasafi recalls his earlier refutation that existence is not limited to the three categories. The division is not a definition, and these concepts are mutually incompatible in one locus. Therefore, God transcends all such categories.
This step shows that the classification itself is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية وهي أنه تعالى لما لم يكن عرضا ولا جوهرا فلو لم يوصف بكونه جسما لالتحق بالعدم، لانحصار الموجودات على هذه الأقسام. فنقول لهم: بم تنفصلون من النصارى حيث يزعمون أنه لو لم يوصف بكونه جوهرا مع استحالة كونه عرضا وجسما لالتحق بالعدم؟ فإن قالوا: بينا القول بالجوهر لأن الجوهر اسم لما هو أصل يتركب منه الجسم أو لأنه قابل للمتضادات، وكل ذلك من أمارات الحدث. فإذا بطل القول بالجوهر والعرض لم يبق إلا الجسم. قيل لهم: بم تنفصلون عنهم حيث يزعمون أن الدليل قام أن كون الموجود جسما من أمارات الحدث على ما قررنا، وما هو من أمارات الحدث منفي عن القديم فلا وجه إلى القول بأنه جسم، فيجب القول بأنه جوهر لئلا يؤدي إلى القول بعدمه، لانحصار الموجودات على هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. فلما جاز لكم أن تقولوا: إنه جسم أن الجسمية من أمارات الحدث، جاز للنصارى أن يقولوا: إنه جوهر مع أن الجوهرية من أمارات الحدث؟ ثم نقول لهم: لما لم يجز القول بالجوهر لما فيه من إثبات الحدث، لم يجز القول بالجسم لما فيه من أمارات الحدث، لأنهما جميعا استويا في علة المنع. ثم نقول لهم - وهو الكشف عن حقيقة المسألة: إنه تعالى لما عرف ثبوته بالدلائل الضرورية وعرف أيضا تبرأته من أمارات الحدث، لأن القول بحدوث القديم محال، وإثبات أمارات الحدث مع انتفاء الحدث وثبوت ما يقابله من القدم محال، ولو جاز ذا لجاز القول بقدم العالم مع ثبوت أمارات الحدث، وحيث لم يجز لما فيه من تعطيل الأدلة وإبطالها ولحوق بالسوفسطائية المتجاهلة، لم يجز القول بذلك في الصانع، وإذا لم يجز دل أن أمارات الحدث عنه منتفية، وكونه جسما أو جوهرا أو عرضا من أمارات الحدث على ما قررنا، فكان ذلك منتفيا عنه ضرورة انتفاء أمارات الحدث، ولا يوجب انتفاؤها انتفاء الذات لقيام الدليل على وجوده. ثم نقول: قد سبق منا القول في إبطال قول النصارى: أنه ليس من ضرورة الموجود أن يكون عرضا لوجود ما ليس بعرض، ولا أن يكون جسما لوجود ما ليس بجسم، ولا أن يكون جوهرا لوجود ما ليس بجوهر، ولا وجه إلى القول بأن الموجود ما يكون عرضا أو جوهرا أو جسما لما أنه تقسیم وليس بتحديد، ولا وجه إلى جمع هذه المعاني في محل لتنافيها، ولا وجه إلى
And as for the second sophism: that since the Exalted is neither an accident nor a substance, then if He were not described as a body, He would be joined with non-existence, because existents are limited to these categories. So we say to them: How do you distinguish yourselves from the Christians, who claim that if He were not described as a substance, given the impossibility of His being an accident or a body, He would be joined with non-existence? If they say: We have explained the assertion of substance because substance is a name for that which is a root from which the body is composed, or because it is receptive of opposites, and all of that is among the marks of origination. So when the assertion of substance and accident is invalidated, nothing remains except the body. It is said to them: How do you distinguish yourselves from them, when they claim that the proof has established that a being's being a body is among the marks of origination, as we have established, and what is among the marks of origination is negated of the Eternal, so there is no aspect to saying that He is a body; rather, it is necessary to say that He is a substance, lest it lead to saying He is non-existent, because existents are limited to these three categories. So if it is permissible for you to say that He is a body, even though corporeality is among the marks of origination, then it is permissible for the Christians to say that He is a substance, even though substantiality is among the marks of origination? Then we say to them: Since it is not permissible to say substance because of the affirmation of origination it entails, it is not permissible to say body because of the marks of origination it entails, for both are equal in the cause of prohibition. Then we say to them—and this is the uncovering of the reality of the matter: Since the Exalted's existence is known by necessary proofs, and His being free from marks of origination is also known, because saying that the Eternal is originated is impossible, and affirming marks of origination while origination is negated and the opposite, which is eternity, is established, is impossible—and if this were permissible, it would be permissible to say that the world is eternal while marks of origination are established; but since that is not permissible because of the nullification and invalidation of proofs it entails, and because it would join the ignorant sophists, it is not permissible to say that of the Maker. And if it is not permissible, it indicates that marks of origination are negated of Him; and His being a body, a substance, or an accident is among the marks of origination, as we have established. So that is negated of Him necessarily due to the negation of marks of origination, and their negation does not necessitate negation of the essence, because the proof for His existence stands. Then we say: We have already stated the refutation of the Christians' claim: that it is not necessary for an existent to be an accident, because there exists what is not an accident; nor to be a body, because there exists what is not a body; nor to be a substance, because there exists what is not a substance. And there is no aspect to saying that the existent is what is an accident, substance, or body, because that is a division, not a definition; and there is no aspect to gathering these meanings in a locus because of their mutual incompatibility; and there is no aspect to...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second sophism (shubha). The objection claims that since God is neither substance (jawhar) nor accident (ʻaraḍ), if He is not also described as a body (jism), He would be non-existent, because all existents are divided into these three categories. The author first responds by asking how the opponents distinguish themselves from Christians, who similarly argue that if God is not a substance, He would be non-existent. The opponents might reply that they affirm substance because it is the root of composition or receptive of opposites—both marks of origination—and since substance and accident are invalid for God, only body remains. But the author turns the argument: the Christians could equally argue that being a body is a mark of origination, so God cannot be a body; to avoid non-existence, He must be a substance. So both sides commit the same fallacy. The author then states that since both body and substance are marks of origination (amārāt al-ḥudūth), neither can be attributed to God; they are equal in the cause of prohibition. Then the author uncovers the true method: God's existence is known by necessary proofs, and His freedom from marks of origination is also known. Affirming such marks would imply that the Eternal is originated, which is absurd. Just as it is impossible to affirm marks of origination for the world while claiming it is eternal, so too for the Creator. Therefore, all marks of origination—including being body, substance, or accident—are negated of God. Their negation does not negate His essence, because proof of His existence stands. The author concludes by recalling the earlier refutation of Christians: existence is not necessarily limited to those three categories; there are existents that are none of them. The division is not a definition, and these concepts are mutually incompatible in one locus.

Key Terms

جسم
جوهر
عرض
أمارات الحدوث
قديم
نصارى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that denying corporeality leads to non-existence because all existents are either substance, accident, or body.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this classification is not exhaustive (there are existents that are none) and that the argument self-destructs when applied consistently, as Christians could equally demand substance. The true principle is that marks of origination cannot apply to the Eternal.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the claim that God must be a body because existents are limited to three categories?
A) By arguing that God is beyond all categories
B) By showing that the same reasoning would require Christians to affirm substance, which they deny
C) By asserting that God is actually an accident
D) By proving that bodies are eternal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that God's existence does not depend on fitting into created categories, and that all marks of origination—whether bodily or substantial—cannot be applied to the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him with human-like attributes (e.g., shape, location, emotions like ours)? How does this passage expose the danger of such thinking? What one step will you take today to replace that imagination with awe of His transcendence?
?
The opponents were so certain that God must fit their categories that they argued against clear reasoning. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that are based on cultural assumptions or emotional comfort rather than evidence from revelation and sound reason? Will you examine them honestly and be willing to let them go?
?
Nasafi shows that denying marks of origination does not negate God's existence—it affirms His perfection. Do you ever fear that rejecting a popular notion about God (e.g., literally sitting on a throne) might lead you to doubt His existence? How does this lesson strengthen your trust in the proofs of His existence over human categories?

Refutation of the Karramiyyah: Existence Does Not Admit Increase
Author refutes Karramiyyah by showing existence does not admit increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence (al-wujud) and self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) cannot admit increase or variation, and how this refutes the Karramiyyah's position.

Core Idea
Existence is beyond increase—it is not like knowledge or power.

The author, Imam al-Nasafi, refutes the Karramiyyah sect by showing that existence and self-subsistence are not subject to increase or variation. He uses the analogy of knowledge to illustrate that only attributes that can increase admit exaggeration and preference. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the background
Briefly note that the Karramiyyah sect held that God's existence could be associated with spatial location or contact with the Throne. This lesson refutes that by showing existence does not admit increase.
The refutation is based on the impossibility of increase in existence itself.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: statement of corruption, the key premise, then the analogy.
The passage ends abruptly, but the core argument is complete.
3
Analyze the premise
Identify the claim: existence and self-subsistence cannot be imagined with increase (tazayyud) or variation (tafavvut). Therefore, exaggeration and preference do not apply.
Exaggeration and preference are only possible for things that increase in themselves.
4
Examine the analogy
Knowledge is given as an example of something that increases. Because it increases, we can say 'He is all-knowing' (exaggeration) and 'Zayd is more knowledgeable than Amr' (preference). Apply this logic to existence.
The analogy reinforces that existence is unlike knowledge; it is perfect and unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فساد قول الكرامية ثم نبين فساد قولهم في هذا فنقول : إن الوجود والقيام بالذات لن يتصور فيها التزايد والتفاوت، فلا تجري فيها المبالغة والتفضيل إذ جريانهما من خصائص ما له تزايد في نفسه ألا ترى أن العلم لما كان متزايدا في نفسه يجري فيه المبالغة فيقال: هو عليم. ويجري فيه التفضيل فيقال : زيد أعلم من عمرو؟ وكذا القدرة والسمع
The corruption of the Karramiyyah's statement. Then we explain the corruption of their statement in this, and we say: Existence (al-wujud) and self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-dhat) cannot be imagined to have increase and variation, so exaggeration and preferability do not apply to them, since their application is from the properties of what has increase in itself. Do you not see that knowledge, because it is increasing in itself, exaggeration applies to it, so it is said: 'He is all-knowing (alim).' And preferability applies to it, so it is said: 'Zayd is more knowledgeable than Amr.' And likewise power and hearing.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyyah sect. The author states the corruption of their view. The argument is that existence (wujud) and self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-dhat) do not admit increase or variation (tazayyud wa tafawut). Therefore, exaggeration (mubalagha) and preference (tafdil) do not apply to them, because these are properties of things that have increase in themselves. For example, knowledge is increasing in itself, so one can say 'He is all-knowing' (exaggeration) and 'Zayd is more knowledgeable than Amr' (preference). Similarly for power and hearing. The implication is that God's existence is not subject to these, so the Karramiyyah's claim of contact with the Throne or other corporeal attributes that imply quantifiable greatness are invalid. The refutation is incomplete in this window, but the core logical premise is presented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since God is described as 'alim' and 'qadir', these imply increase in His attributes as they do in creation.
Correction: The author distinguishes between attributes that admit increase (like knowledge in creation) and God's existence and attributes which are absolutely perfect and do not admit increase. The terms 'alim' and 'qadir' for God indicate perfection, not increase.

Checkpoint

B) Because existence does not admit increase or variation.
C) Because exaggeration only applies to physical objects.
D) Because existence is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Existence (al-wujud) does not admit increase; therefore, any attribute that implies augmentation cannot apply to Allah's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that Allah's existence is beyond increase or comparison. When you look at your own pursuit of closeness to Allah, do you measure your progress by comparing yourself to others? How can you shift your focus to simply being present with the One who has no increase or decrease?
?
The author uses the example of knowledge increasing in creation. Reflect: Is your search for Islamic knowledge driven by a desire to be 'more knowledgeable' than others, or by a need to know the One whose existence is absolute? What step can you take today to purify your intention?
?
If existence itself cannot admit increase, then Allah's attributes are perfect without any addition. What does this mean for your concept of earning 'more' reward or attaining 'higher' ranks? Does your spiritual ambition rest on comparison or on surrendering to the All-Perfect?

Refuting 'Agent Implies Body'
Author refutes the claim that being an agent necessitates being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that God must be a body because He is an agent, by exposing the ambiguity of the term 'body'.

Core Idea
Being an agent does not require being a body; the analogy from creation is flawed.

Opponents of kalām sometimes argue that since God is described as an agent (fa'il) in revelation, and agents in the visible world are bodies, God must be a body. The author of Tabsirat al-Adilla systematically dismantles this argument by asking a simple question: What exactly do you mean by 'body'? The passage that follows shows how the objection collapses under its own ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how the author questions the opponent's definition of 'body'.
The passage is incomplete in the source—it ends with 'alā an'—but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the three possible meanings
The author presents three possible meanings of 'body': (1) composite/divisible, (2) existent/self-subsistent, (3) something else called 'body'. Find each in the text.
3
Analyze the refutations
For each meaning, note how the author shows it fails: the first contradicts the opponent's own school, the second equates body with existence (invalid), the third is already refuted. Also note the extra point about inanimate objects (جماد).
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this refutation is effective: it forces the opponent to clarify terms and then shows that no valid meaning of 'body' leads to the desired conclusion.
This is a classic kalām technique: dissect the analogical premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تقولون: إنه فاعل فيكون جسما استدلالا بالشاهد فنقول ايش تعنون أن كل فاعل في الشاهد جسم أتعنون أنه متركب متبعض أم أنه موجود وقائم بالذات أم أنه يسمى جسما؟ فإن عنيتم أنه متركب متبعض تركتم مذهبكم والتحقتم بالفريق الأول. وإن عنيتم أنه موجود أو قائم بالذات، فنحن نساعدكم ونقول: إنه لما كان فاعلا كان موجودا وقائما بالذات، إلا أن جعل الجسم اسما للموجود أو القائم بالذات أنه جسما فهو فاسد، لأنا لو سمينا عرضا جسما لا يسمى مر. وإن عنيتم باطل لما مر. يجوز منه الفعل، وكذا الجماد يسمى جسما ويستحيل منه الفعل فدل أنه باطل على أن
And what you say: He is an agent (فاعل), so He must be a body, inferring from the witnessed. We say: What do you mean? Do you mean that every agent in the witnessed is a body, meaning that it is composed and divisible? Or that it exists and subsists by itself? Or that it is called a body? If you mean it is composed and divisible, you leave your school and join the first group. If you mean it exists or subsists by itself, then we agree with you and say: Since He is an agent, He exists and subsists by itself. But making 'body' a name for the existent or self-subsistent is invalid, because if we call an accident (عرض) a body it would not be called... And if you mean something else, it is invalid for the previous reason. Also, an inanimate object (جماد) is called a body but action from it is impossible, so this proves it is invalid regarding...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that because God is an agent (fa'il), He must be a body like agents in the visible world. The author dissects the meaning of 'body': if it means composite/divisible, the objectors contradict their own school; if it means existent/self-subsistent, then they are equating 'body' with existence, which is invalid—accidents exist but are not bodies. Moreover, inanimate objects are called bodies but cannot act. The argument is thus invalid, though the text is incomplete at the end.

Key Terms

موجود
قائم بالذات
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being an agent (fā'il) logically requires being a body, because all agents in the visible world are bodies.
Correction: The term 'body' is ambiguous. If it means composite/divisible, that contradicts other theological commitments. If it means existent/self-subsistent, then God is that, but that does not entail 'body' in the physical sense. Moreover, inanimate bodies cannot act, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that God must be a body because He is an agent?
A) By showing that 'body' has multiple meanings and the analogy fails.
B) By denying that God is an agent.
C) By saying all agents are bodies.
D) By citing scripture.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God must be a body because He is an agent is refuted by showing that the analogical inference relies on an ambiguous term 'body' and fails under scrutiny.

The Dialectical Argument for a Creator and the Objective Basis of Similarity
Al-Nasafi challenges attribute deniers with a question about the Creator and defines similarity by meaning not words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi’s dialectical method to expose the self-contradiction of those who deny divine attributes, and his argument that similarity is based on objective meaning, not words.

Core Idea
Similarity is rooted in meaning, not language—words are merely indicators.

Al-Nasafi begins by cornering those who reject divine attributes with a simple question: does the world have a creator? Their answer—yes or no—leads to a dilemma. He then clarifies that similarity between Creator and creatures is not determined by the words we use but by the actual meanings of attributes. This foundational principle helps refute claims that describing God by attributes implies anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the argument unfolds as a dialogue between the author and the opponent.
Pay attention to the two distinct sections: the dialectical challenge and the definition of similarity.
2
Identify the two-part structure
The passage has a dialectical challenge (the question and dilemma) followed by a conceptual clarification (the nature of similarity). Mark where the transition happens.
The transition occurs at 'ثم نقول لهم'.
3
Understand the dilemma
If the opponent says 'No', they reveal atheism. If they say 'Yes', they are forced to describe the Creator, which contradicts their denial of positive attributes. Any answer destroys their position.
This is a classic dialectical trap that exposes inconsistency.
4
Grasp the definition of similarity
Al-Nasafi argues that similarity is known by objective meaning, not by words. Even if no word is used, we recognize similarity or dissimilarity through observation.
Names are just indicators (dalat); they do not create similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : هل للعالم صانع ? فإن قالوا : لا . فقد أظهروا ما هو مكنون سريرتهم من تعطيل الصانع ونفيه، وإن قالوا : نعم. قيل لهم: من هو ? وبأي اسم تعرفونه ? وبأي صفة تصفونه ? ومن الموصوف بالوجود عندكم ? ومن الذي منه بدأ العالم العلوي والسفلي ? ومن الذي أبدع العقل والنفس فسميتموها المبدع الأول، والمبدع الثاني ? فإن أجابوا شيء هدموا أصلهم وإن سكتوا كفينا مؤنة مجادلتهم. ثم نقول لهم : المماثلة ليست بمأخوذة من تقدير اللسان، وإطلاق الاسم والقول بل هي ثابتة في المعنى، وإن لم يطلق عليه قول، فإنا إذا رأينا شيئين متماثلين عرفنا تماثلهما، وإن لم نسمع قولا يطلق عليهما، وإذا رأيناهما غير متماثلين عرفناهما كذلك وإن أطلق عليهما قول واحد، وهذا لأن الأسامي دالات
And it is said to them: Does the world have a creator? If they say: No, they have revealed what is hidden in their hearts of denying the Creator and negating Him. If they say: Yes, it is said to them: Who is He? By what name do you know Him? By what attribute do you describe Him? Who is described by existence according to you? From whom did the upper and lower world originate? Who originated the intellect and the soul, which you call the First Originator and the Second Originator? If they answer anything, they demolish their own foundation, and if they remain silent, we are spared the trouble of disputing with them. Then we say to them: Similarity is not derived from linguistic convention or the application of a name or word; rather, it is established in meaning, even if no word is applied. For we, when we see two similar things, we know their similarity even if we have not heard a word applied to them; and when we see two dissimilar things, we know them as dissimilar even if a single word is applied to them. This is because names are indicators.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (objection-and-answer) against those who deny divine attributes. The author first poses a key question: 'Does the world have a creator?' If the opponent denies it, they reveal their atheism. If they affirm it, they are pressed to specify who this creator is, by what name and attribute they know Him, etc. If they provide any answer (e.g., the First Mover, the intellect), they contradict their own denial of positive attributes. If they remain silent, the case collapses. Then, the author pivots to define similarity (tamathul) objectively: similarity is based on inherent meaning, not on the words used. Even if no word is uttered, we recognize similarity or dissimilarity by observation. Names (asma') are merely indicators (dalat), not the reality of similarity. Thus, the deniers' reliance on avoiding certain words is futile because similarity is a matter of meaning, not vocabulary. The argument underscores that attributing existence, life, etc., to God does not imply likeness to creatures when understood correctly.

Key Terms
صانع
تعطيل
مماثلة
أسامي
دلالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think similarity between God and creatures is determined by the words used; if the same word is applied, it implies resemblance.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that similarity is based on objective meaning, not on verbal labels. Two things can be similar in meaning even if different words are used, and two things can be dissimilar even if the same word is applied.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the question 'Does the world have a creator?' accomplish?
A) It proves the world is eternal
B) It forces the opponent either to admit atheism or to contradict their own position
C) It shows similarity is verbal
D) It proves God has no attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that denying divine attributes either leads to atheism or self-contradiction, and that similarity is a matter of meaning, not vocabulary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided describing Allah with attributes like ‘anger’ or ‘mercy’ because you feared it would make Him seem like creation? Did this passage show you that such fear is based on a misunderstanding of similarity? How will you now respond when your heart hesitates to affirm His attributes?
?
When you say ‘Allah exists,’ do you simply repeat the words, or do you truly grasp that His existence is absolutely unique, unlike any created existence? What prevents you from affirming His attributes with full conviction? What would change in your worship if you internalized that His life, knowledge, and power are nothing like yours?
?
Consider your own use of language about Allah. Are there times you avoid certain words or descriptions out of discomfort? After reading this passage, what specific attribute of Allah will you now affirm more boldly, and what practical step will you take today to remind yourself that words are merely indicators, not the reality of His perfection?

Forcing the Batiniyya to Admit a Creator
Nasafi interrogates the Batiniyya about the world's maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a forced-choice question to expose the Batiniyya's denial or force them to contradict their own principles.

Core Idea
Admitting a creator forces description; denying reveals atheism.

This passage demonstrates Nasafi's dialectical method. By asking a simple yes/no question about the world's maker, he corners the Batiniyya into revealing their true stance. If they admit a creator, they must describe Him—contradicting their aversion to names and attributes. If they deny, they expose their hidden atheism. This lesson shows how kalam uses logical pressure to dismantle opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the initial question and the two possible answers.
Notice how the question is phrased to force a binary choice.
2
Examine the follow-up questions
If the Batiniyya say 'yes', Nasafi asks for the creator's identity, name, and attributes. List these questions in your mind.
These questions target their core principle of avoiding names.
3
Understand the trap
Any answer to these questions would violate the Batiniyya's own doctrine. Silence also counts as defeat.
Nasafi concludes that they either demolish their principle or we are spared debate.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this forced-choice is effective: it exposes internal inconsistency without external assumptions.
This is a common kalam tactic for refuting opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: هل للعالم صانع؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد أظهروا ما هو مكنون سريرتهم من تعطيل الصانع ونفيه، وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل لهم: من هو؟ وبأي اسم تعرفونه؟ وبأي صفة تصفونه؟ ومن الموصوف بالوجود عندكم؟ ومن الذي منه بدأ العالم العلوي والسفلي؟ ومن الذي أبدع العقل والنفس فسميتموها المبدع الأول، والمبدع الثاني؟ فإن أجابوا شيء هدموا أصلهم وإن سكتوا كفينا مؤنة مجادلتهم. ثم نقول لهم: المماثلة ليست بمأخوذة من تقدير اللسان، وإطلاق الاسم والقول بل هي ثابتة في المعنى، وإن لم يطلق عليه قول، فإنا إذا رأينا شيئين متماثلين عرفنا تماثلهما، وإن لم نسمع قولا يطلق عليهما، وإذا رأيناهما غير متماثلين عرفناهما كذلك وإن أطلق عليهما قول واحد، وهذا لأن الأسامي دالات على حقائق المسميات، وأحوالها، وأوصافها، والدليل لا أثر له في المدلول إلا بالإظهار، فأما الوجود أو التغير فلا يتعلق بالدليل ألا ترى أنا لو رأينا بياضين فسمينا أحدهما باسم والآخر باسم آخر لا تثبت بينهما مخالفة بمخالفة الاسم ولا تزول المماثلة الثابتة بمخالفة الاسم، ولو سمينا مختلفين أو متضادين باسم واحد لا يوجب ذلك التماثل بينهما وارتفاع المخالفة الثابتة بينهما ليصيرا متماثلين من جميع الوجوه بثبوت موافقة في الاسم بحيث لا مخالفة بينهما فيه، وإذا كان كذلك يقال لهم هل بين من تنسبون إليه ثبوت العالم وبين شيء من العالم مماثلة في المعنى؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد وقعوا في شيء مما أبوا لأنهم دفعوا الاشتراك في الاسم لئلا يقع به التشبيه من حيث الاسم، وأثبتوا التشبيه في المعنى، وإن قالوا: لا. قلنا لهم ونحن نساعدكم على هذا فيعد ذلك بإطلاق اسم يطلق مثل ذلك الاسم على غيره لا يتغير عما كان عليه ولا تثبت به الموافقة.
And it is said to them: Does the world have a maker? If they say: No, then they have revealed what is hidden in their hearts of denying the maker and negating Him. And if they say: Yes, it is said to them: Who is He? And by what name do you know Him? And by what attribute do you describe Him? And who is the one described by existence according to you? And from whom did the upper and lower world originate? And who originated the intellect and the soul, which you call the first originator and the second originator? So if they answer anything, they demolish their own principle; and if they remain silent, we are spared the trouble of debating them. Then we say to them: Similarity is not derived from the estimation of the tongue and the utterance of names and speech, but rather it is established in meaning, even if no statement is uttered about it. For if we see two similar things, we know their similarity even if we have not heard a statement uttered about them; and if we see them dissimilar, we know them to be so even if a single statement is uttered about them. This is because names indicate the realities of the named things, their states, and their attributes, and the indicator has no effect on the indicated except by way of manifestation. As for existence or change, it is not connected to the indicator. Do you not see that if we saw two white things and called one by a name and the other by another name, no difference would be established between them by the difference of the name, nor would the established similarity be removed by the difference of the name? And if we called two different or opposite things by a single name, that would not necessitate similarity between them or the removal of the established difference between them so that they become similar in all respects by the establishment of agreement in the name, such that there is no difference between them in it. And if that is the case, it is said to them: Is there between the one to whom you attribute the existence of the world and anything of the world similarity in meaning? If they say: Yes, then they have fallen into something they abhor, because they repudiated sharing in the name lest anthropomorphism occur through the name, and they affirmed anthropomorphism in meaning. And if they say: No, we say to them—and we assist you in this—that then, by applying a name that is applied like that name to another, it does not change from what it was, nor does agreement become established by it.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage contains two main arguments. First, the author forces the Batiniyya to admit that the world has a maker. He poses a series of questions: if they deny a maker, they reveal their true atheism; if they affirm, they must identify Him by some name and attribute. They cannot escape, because any answer they give will contradict their principle of avoiding names. Then the author moves to a deeper discussion about similarity (tamathul). He argues that similarity is based on the meaning of things, not on the names used for them. Names are mere indicators; they do not change the reality of the thing. Therefore, if someone denies a name to avoid implying similarity, that denial does not prevent actual similarity in meaning if it exists. Conversely, using the same name for two different things does not create similarity. The author then applies this to the Batiniyya: if they say the Creator has no similarity to anything in the world (in meaning), then using names like 'existent' for both does not create similarity; if they say there is similarity, then they have fallen into tashbih despite avoiding names. Either way, their position is inconsistent. This is a sophisticated logical argument defending the use of names for God while avoiding anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
صانع
تعطيل
مبدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to acknowledge a creator without describing Him in any way.
Correction: In kalam, acknowledging a creator logically implies some description (e.g., existence), so avoiding all names is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What happens if the Batiniyya answer the follow-up questions about the creator?
A) They prove the creator's existence
B) They demolish their own principle
C) They remain consistent
D) They avoid anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's forced-choice argument shows that either denial of creation or admission without description is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in your heart, tried to reduce Allah to a vague concept to avoid the responsibility of knowing Him? This passage shows that silence is not safe—it is defeat. What will you actively do today to learn one of Allah's names or attributes and apply it in your life?
?
Imagine you are asked, 'Does the world have a maker?' Could you respond with the same clarity as Nasafi, or would you hesitate? What is one doubt about Allah's existence or description that you have avoided confronting? Commit to writing it down and seeking a clear answer from the Quran and Sunnah this week.

Similarity is Based on Meaning, Not Names
Nasafi explains that similarity is based on meaning, not names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity between two things is determined by their intrinsic meanings, not by the names or words used to describe them.

Core Idea
Names don't create similarity; meanings do.

After cornering the Batiniyya, Nasafi addresses their fear of anthropomorphism. They avoid using names for God to prevent implying similarity with creation. In this passage, he argues that similarity is a matter of meaning, not nomenclature. Using clear examples, he shows that names are mere indicators that do not alter the reality of things. This lesson clarifies the relationship between names and meanings, and applies it to defend the use of divine names without falling into anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
Read the first sentence: 'المماثلة ليست بمأخوذة من تقدير اللسان...' Understand the claim that similarity is in meaning.
Note the phrase 'ثابتة في المعنى' – established in meaning.
2
Examine the examples
The passage gives two examples: two white things with different names, and two opposite things with the same name. Analyze what these show about names and similarity.
The first example shows name difference doesn't create difference; the second shows same name doesn't create similarity.
3
Understand the application
Nasafi applies this to the Batiniyya's claim about God and the world. He poses a question about similarity in meaning.
Their answer determines whether they fall into anthropomorphism or not.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Either they admit similarity in meaning (contradicting their avoidance) or they deny it (making the shared name harmless).
This shows the Batiniyya's position is inconsistent or ineffective.

Proving God's Knowledge and Power through Creation
Nasafi proves God's knowledge and power by questioning the Batiniyya about creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses dialectical questions about creation to establish that God possesses knowledge, power, and free will.

Core Idea
Creation implies the Creator's knowledge, power, and freedom.

Nasafi engages in a hypothetical debate with the Batiniyya, using logical questions to draw out the necessary attributes of God. By examining what creation requires, he demonstrates that God must be knowing, powerful, and free. This lesson walks through each step of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: if they say X, then Y follows.
2
Trace the argument's dialectical structure
Notice how Nasafi poses a series of yes/no questions. Each answer leads either to an absurd consequence (if they deny) or to affirming an attribute (if they agree).
This is a classic kalam method called ilzam (forcing a conclusion).
3
Identify the attributes established
The passage establishes God's action (fi'l), knowledge ('ilm), power (qudra), and volition (ikhtiyar). See how each is proven.
The final sentence summarizes the three attributes: existent, knowing, powerful.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why proving these attributes is essential for Islamic theology. Without them, God would be described as ignorant or powerless, which contradicts His divinity.
This argument shows that attributes are not separate from essence but are necessary implications of His acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: هل فعل هذا العالم وأحدثه؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد عطلوه. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: وهل علم قبل وجود العالم أنه يوجده؟ وعلى أي وصف يوجده؟ وبعدما أوجده هل يعلم أن العالم موجود وأنه أوجده؟ فإن قالوا: لا جهلوه، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد ثبت علمه. ثم قيل لهم: هل كان مضطرا في خلق العالم؟ أم كان مختارا؟ فإن قالوا: كان مضطرا. فقد عجزوه ووصفوه بما ينافي القدم. وإن قالوا كان مختارا قيل: وهل يكون المختار غير قادر؟ فإن قالوا: نعم ظهر عنادهم، وإن قالوا: لا. قيل: ثبتت قدرته. ثم بعد ثبوت فعله وعلمه، وقدرته كان الوصف له بأنه موجود، عالم، قادر،
Then we say to them: Did He do this world and originate it? If they say: No, then they have denied Him. And if they say: Yes, it is said: Did He know before the existence of the world that He would bring it into existence? And upon what description would He bring it into existence? And after He brought it into existence, does He know that the world exists and that He brought it into existence? If they say: No, they have rendered Him ignorant; and if they say: Yes, then His knowledge is established. Then it is said to them: Was He compelled in creating the world, or was He free? If they say: He was compelled, then they have rendered Him powerless and described Him with what contradicts pre-eternity. And if they say: He was free, it is said: Does the free agent be other than powerful? If they say: Yes, their obstinacy appears; and if they say: No, it is said: His power is established. Then after the establishment of His action, knowledge, and power, the description for Him is that He is existent, knowing, powerful.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a series of dialectical questions aimed at establishing that God possesses the attributes of knowledge, power, and will (or freedom). The author starts by asking whether God created the world. If the opponent denies creation, they are accused of atheism (ta'til). If they affirm, then the author asks about foreknowledge: did God know before creating? If no, then God is ignorant; if yes, then knowledge is established. Next, the author asks about compulsion: was God compelled in creation? If compelled, then God is powerless and not eternal (since compulsion implies need). If free, then the author asks whether a free agent can be other than powerful. If they say yes, they are obstinate; if no, then power is established. Finally, the author concludes that since action, knowledge, and power are established, God must be described as existent, knowing, and powerful. This is a classic kalam proof for the essential attributes, showing that they are necessary consequences of creation.

Key Terms
فعل
علم
قدرة
اضطرار
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving God's existence does not require proving His attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that attributes like knowledge and power are logically inseparable from creation; denying them leads to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
If the Batiniyya affirm that the world is created, what attributes must they also affirm according to Nasafi?
A) That God is a body
B) That God is knowing and powerful
C) That God is not eternal
D) That God is limited

Key Takeaway
From creation, we deduce that the Creator must be knowing, powerful, and free.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you genuinely acknowledge that every detail is known by God before and after its existence? How does that change the way you view your own life and struggles?
?
If God had been compelled to create you, what would that imply about His perfection? Why does it matter for your worship that He created you by free choice?
?
This passage shows that denying any of God's attributes leads to absurdity. Are there any attributes of God that you are uncomfortable affirming? What stops you from submitting fully to His description of Himself?
